Shahadat — Testimony
advanced
12 min
Special Cases: Guarantor, Settlement, Weight, and Purchase
Presents applications where the rule bends for specific legal relationships.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on testimony discrepancy to special contexts such as guarantor discharge, settlement with same type, weight differences, and spousal transactions.

Core Idea
Testimony is accepted if the difference is due to a change in legal status or if the discrepancy is within customary tolerance.

The Hanafi school recognizes that not all discrepancies are equal. In cases involving guarantees, settlements, or differing weights, the testimony may still be accepted if the substantive right remains intact. This lesson examines those subtle applications from the passage.

Guided Path
1
Examine the guarantor (kafil) case
A guarantor claims payment, and witnesses testify to his discharge (bar'a). This is testimony to a different legal event, not a discrepancy in amount.
The discharge of the guarantor does not affect the principal's liability.
2
Analyze the settlement (sulh) case
If the debtor claims discharge, and witnesses testify that the plaintiff settled for a known amount, the testimony is accepted if the settlement is with the same type as the original right.
Settlement includes partial satisfaction and partial waiver.
3
Understand the weight discrepancy case
If the claim specifies a weight (Samarqand) and witnesses testify with a different weight (Mecca), it is accepted if the Mecca weight is equal or less.
Custom and equivalence matter.
4
Look at the purchase of slave-girl case
The wife claims she bought a slave-girl from her husband for her mahr. Witnesses testify that the husband gave her mahr without a sale. The testimony is accepted because the substance (transfer of ownership) may still be valid.
The exact transaction type is not material if the right is achieved.

الجارية وطلاق المرأة يعرف ذلك بالتأمل في كلامهم.

Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage compiles numerous rulings from several Hanafi fatwa collections (al-Muhit al-Burhani, al-Muhit, al-Bazzaziyya, al-Sirajiyya, al-Qunyah) on the topic of discrepancy between the claim (da'wa) and the testimony (shahada). The overarching principle: testimony is accepted if it does not exceed the claim; if it exceeds, it is rejected unless the plaintiff explicitly disowns the excess. However, if the testimony falls short of the claim (e.g., witnesses testify to a lesser amount), it is generally accepted for that lesser amount. The examples cover: (1) Building on a house: denial of ownership of the building after judgment invalidates if witnesses mentioned it. (2) Partial acknowledgment of receipt: if it predates the claim, it is denial. (3) Multiple defendants: if plaintiff confirms testimony for one but denies for another, both are invalidated because it shows untruth. (4) Inheritance: a brother's statement that he has no share affects distribution. (5) Bequest and relationship: conflict between two claims. (6) Guarantor's discharge: testimony accepted for the discharge but does not affect principal. (7) Amount vs. sum: testimony for 'mablagh' (amount) might be rejected if ambiguous. (8) Settlement (sulh): if settlement is with same type, testimony accepted. (9) Weight discrepancy: if witnesses mention a different weight, it's accepted if equal or less. (10) Purchase of slave-girl: testimony about mahr without sale is accepted. The passage concludes with a note that there are 23 exceptional cases where testimony despite discrepancy is accepted, but in reality they are not exceptions because the testified amount is less than the claim.

Key Terms
الكفيل (al-kafil): Guarantor; one who stands surety.
الصُلْح (al-sulh): Settlement; compromise where parties agree to end dispute by mutual concession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A wife claims divorce by husband's agent. Witnesses testify that the husband divorced her himself. What is the ruling?
A) The testimony is rejected because it does not match the claim.
B) The divorce occurs regardless of the method.
C) The wife must prove agency.
D) The witnesses are considered liars.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi law accepts testimony that differs from the claim in legal characterization if the substantive right is achieved—a flexible approach that prioritizes justice over rigid formalism.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate

Testimony Void When It Exceeds the Claim
Examples of testimony being rejected when it exceeds the claim or when the claimant denies part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that testimony exceeding the claim is void, and apply it to distinguish between valid and void testimony scenarios.

Core Idea
Testimony that exceeds the claim is void; testimony that is less or matches is accepted.

In Hanafi testimony law, the principle is simple: witnesses cannot say more than what the claimant asserts. If they do, their entire testimony collapses. This lesson explores numerous applications of this rule, from multi-defendant cases to subtle differences in quantity, weight, and legal description. Understanding this principle prevents procedural errors in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the testimony
Determine exactly what the claimant alleged and exactly what the witnesses stated. The comparison begins here.
2
Compare the two statements
Check if the testimony matches, exceeds, or falls short of the claim in terms of subject matter, quantity, or legal description.
Excess means more than claimed; less means same or inferior.
3
Apply the rule
If the testimony exceeds the claim, it is void (bāṭil). If it is equal or less, it is accepted (maqbūl).
This is the core Hanafi principle from the passage.
4
Note special cases where apparent excess is actually equivalent
Custom may treat 'amount' as quantity, or different weights may be equal, making testimony acceptable despite apparent discrepancy.
Example: 'mablagh' in custom means quantity, not a different type of money.
5
Recognize the 'exceptions' as not true exceptions
The passage lists 23 scenarios where testimony is accepted despite discrepancy. In reality, they are not exceptions because the testimony is less than the claim in each case.
The author clarifies that the prohibited discrepancy is only when testimony is more.

له بمبلغ عشرة آلاف درهم لم تقبل لأن مبلغ هذا المال مال آخر . شهدا على دعوى أرض أنها خمسة مكاييل وأصابا في بيان حدودها وأخطآ في المقدار قبلت ا هـ . وفي العرف أن المبلغ هو القدر فإنهم يقولون قبض مبلغ كذا أي قدر كذا لا مال آخر فينبغي أن تقبل الشهادة في عرفنا. وفي القنية: ادعى المديون الإيصال إلى الدائن متفرقاً وشهد شهوده بالإيصال مطلقاً أو جملة لا تقبل. ادعت على زوجها أنه وكل وكيلاً فطلقني وشهدا أنه طلقها بنفسه يقع الطلاق. ادعت الطلاق فشهدا لأن بالخلع تسمع وجه التوفيق ممكن، ولو ادعى المديون الإبراء وشهدوا أن المدعي صالح المدعى عليه بمال معلوم تقبل شهادتهم إن كان الصلح بجنس الحق لحصول الإبراء عن البعض بالاستيفاء عن البعض بالإسقاط، ولو ادعى عليه خمسة دنانير بوزن سمرقند فشهدوا فسألهم القاضي عن الوزن فقالوا بوزن مكة تقبل شهادتهم إن كان وزن مكة مثل وزن سمرقند أو أقل وإلا فلا ادعت أنها اشترت هذه الجارية من زوجها بمهرها وشهدوا أن زوجها أعطاها مهرها من غير أن يجري البيع بينهما تقبل ا هـ . وبما قررناه علم أن المستثنى من قوله «وإلا لا ثلاث عشرة مسألة وسيأتي قريباً ثمانٍ أخرى في الإقرار والإنشاء، واثنان في المقيد بسبب والمطلق فصارت ثلاثة وعشرين فليتأمل. ثم اعلم أن في الحقيقة لا استثناء لأن المخالفة المانعة أن يكون المشهود به أكثر ففي كل صورة قالوا بالمنع إنما هو لكونه أكثر من المدعي Two witnesses testified for him against a man for a thousand and against another for a hundred; he confirmed them in the first and denied them in the second, both testimonies are void. Similarly, if they testified for seizure of two garments and he confirmed them in one and denied them in the other, both are void. If a judge ruled for three sons regarding inheritance from their father, then one of them said, 'I have no right in it; it is only for my two brothers,' then all of it goes to the two brothers. If he said, 'I had no right in it; it is only for them,' his adjudicated share is void. If he claimed that the testator bequeathed him a thousand dirhams and proved it, then claimed he is the son of the testator without proof, he gets the lesser of the inheritance and the thousand. Muhammad said: The bequest is void and nothing. In al-Bazzaziyya: If the debtor claimed payment, and witnesses testified to the creditor's discharge or that he is solvent, it is accepted, just as if he claimed seizure and witnesses testified to an admission of it, it is accepted. If the guarantor (kafil) claimed payment, and witnesses testified to discharge, it is accepted. The scenario is placed on payment to indicate that payment is not restricted to it; hence the guarantor does not have recourse against the principal, but the claimant has recourse against the principal, as if it is a discharge of the guarantor, and discharge of the guarantor does not require discharge of the principal. He mentioned it only to indicate that what is adjudged is the discharge of the guarantor, not payment. This is because the guarantor's claim includes discharge while he has the ability to recourse against the principal, and his two witnesses testified to part of his claim definitively, so it is accepted in that part, not in the excess. End. In al-Sirajiyya: If he claimed ten thousand dirhams, and two witnesses testified for him to an amount of ten thousand dirhams, it is not accepted because 'the amount of this money' is other money. If they testified to a claim of land that it is five measures, and they were correct in stating its boundaries but erred in the quantity, it is accepted. End. In custom, 'the amount' means the quantity; they say 'he received such-and-such amount' i.e., such-and-such quantity, not other money, so testimony should be accepted in our custom. In al-Quniyya: If the debtor claimed delivery to the creditor in installments, and his witnesses testified to delivery absolutely or in lump sum, it is not accepted. If a woman claimed against her husband that he authorized an agent to divorce me, and witnesses testified that he divorced her himself, the divorce occurs. If she claimed divorce, and witnesses testified for her to khul', it is heard because reconciliation is possible. If the debtor claimed discharge, and witnesses testified that the claimant settled with the defendant for a known amount, their testimony is accepted if the settlement was with the same type of right, because discharge occurs for part by receipt and for part by dropping. If he claimed five dinars by Samarkand weight, and witnesses testified; the judge asked them about the weight, and they said 'by Mecca weight'; their testimony is accepted if Mecca weight equals Samarkand weight or is less, otherwise no. If she claimed that she bought this slave-girl from her husband for her mahr, and witnesses testified that her husband gave her her mahr without the sale occurring between them, it is accepted. End. From what we have established, it is known that the exceptions to his statement 'otherwise no' are thirteen issues, and soon eight more will come in iqrar and insha', and two in muqayyad (restricted) and mutlaq (absolute), making twenty-three, so reflect. Then know that in reality there is no exception, because the prohibited discrepancy is that the testified amount is more; in every case they prohibited it only because it is more than the claimed amount; and in every case they accepted it despite discrepancy, it is only because the testified amount is less, and likewise in the emancipation of a slave-girl and divorce of a woman, this is known by reflection on their statements.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This is an expansion probe from the commentary of Kanz al-Daqaiq, presenting a series of rulings on testimony discrepancy. The core principle is that if witnesses testify to something that exceeds the claim in extent or contradicts its basis, the testimony is rejected. The author provides numerous examples: (1) If two witnesses testify for a claimant against two different defendants for different amounts, and the claimant confirms one testimony but denies the other, both testimonies become void because the partial denial implies lying. (2) Similarly for seizure of two garments. (3) If three sons are adjudged inheritance and one later says he has no right, the share returns to the others; but if he says he never had a right, his adjudged share is void. (4) If a claimant proves a bequest of a thousand dirhams and then claims to be the testator's son without proof, he takes the lesser of the inheritance and the bequest; Muhammad al-Shaybani says the bequest is void. (5) In al-Bazzaziyya, scenarios where testimony is accepted: debtor claims payment, witnesses testify to discharge; guarantor claims payment, witnesses testify to discharge of the guarantor (not the principal). (6) In al-Sirajiyya, testimony for a specific amount when the claim is for a different amount (like ten thousand vs the amount of ten thousand) is rejected, unless the custom equates 'amount' with quantity. (7) Land testimony: if boundaries are correct but quantity wrong, testimony is accepted. (8) In al-Quniyya, testimony of absolute delivery when claimant alleged installments is rejected. (9) Wife claims husband authorized someone to divorce; witnesses say he divorced himself – divorce occurs because the result is same. (10) Wife claims divorce, witnesses say khul' (divorce by compensation) – accepted because reconciliation possible. (11) Debtor claims discharge, witnesses say settlement for known amount with same type – accepted. (12) Weight difference: if claimed by Samarkand weight and witnesses testify by Mecca weight, accepted if the weights are equal or lesser. (13) Woman claims she bought slave-girl from husband for mahr, witnesses say husband gave mahr without sale – accepted because the essence is different. The author then summarizes that exceptions to the rule 'otherwise no' are 13, plus 8 from iqrar and insha', and 2 from muqayyad and mutlaq, totaling 23. He clarifies that in truth there is no exception because the disallowed discrepancy is when the testimony exceeds the claim; whenever testimony is less, it is accepted.

Key Terms
بطلتا (batalatā): Both testimonies become void and invalid.
براءة (barā'ah): Discharge; release from a debt or obligation.
مبلغ (mablagh): Amount; the precise quantity or sum of money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A claimant claims that he bought a slave-girl from his husband for her mahr. Witnesses testify that the husband gave her her mahr without any sale. What is the ruling?
A) Testimony is void because it does not mention a sale.
B) Testimony is accepted because the husband giving the mahr is less than the claim of a sale.
C) Testimony is void because it exceeds the claim.
D) Testimony is accepted only if the witnesses specify the mahr amount.

Key Takeaway
The core Hanafi principle: testimony is void only when it exceeds the claim; otherwise it is accepted. Always compare the quantity, value, or legal description of the testimony to the claim.

Absolute Ownership Testimony Invalid for Claim of Inheritance or Purchase
Commentary on the ruling: testimony of absolute ownership is rejected when claiming inheritance or purchase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why testimony of absolute ownership is rejected when the claim specifies purchase or inheritance, and distinguish the ruling for real property versus debt.

Core Idea
Testimony exceeding the claim is rejected: absolute ownership differs fundamentally from recent acquisition.

In Hanafi law, testimony must align with the claim. When a claimant specifies a cause of ownership (purchase or inheritance), testimony of absolute ownership introduces a contradiction: absolute ownership implies ancient, original possession, while recent acquisition is temporally limited. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and the critical exception for debts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and testimony
The claimant claims a house by purchase or inheritance. Witnesses testify to absolute ownership (mulk mutlaq). Recognize that absolute ownership is not tied to any specific cause.
Absolute ownership means the claimant owned it from the beginning, not through a recent transaction.
2
Recognize the mismatch
Absolute ownership implies ancient ownership with entitlement to increments (zawa'id) and seller recourse. Recent ownership (purchase or inheritance) does not carry these attributes. The two are legally distinct categories.
The difference is substantive, not semantic.
3
Understand the reasoning
The two types of ownership are irreconcilable: the ancient cannot become recent, nor the recent become ancient. Witnesses testified to more than what was claimed, so testimony is rejected.
The author treats inheritance like purchase; the well-known view treats inheritance like absolute ownership.
4
Apply the distinction for debt
For debt (dayn), absolute ownership testimony may be accepted because debt carries no increments (zawa'id). Thus, no contradiction arises. Some Hanafis accept absolute testimony for debt; the commentator prefers acceptance.
This is a point of intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Note the condition for purchase claims
If the claimant states the seller is known, and witnesses testify to absolute ownership, testimony is rejected. But if the seller is unknown (e.g., 'bought from a man'), the testimony is accepted because the claim of purchase from an unknown is a null admission.
Muhammad al-Shaybani's alternative reasoning: claimant admitted ownership to seller, then claimed transfer without proof.

His statement: '(If he claimed a house by inheritance or purchase, and witnesses testified to absolute ownership, it is invalid)' i.e., the evidence is not accepted, because they testified to more than what the claimant claimed. For he claimed recent ownership, and they testified to ancient ownership, and the two are different. Since absolute ownership is established from the beginning such that the claimant becomes entitled to its increments, while this is not the case for recent ownership; and the sellers have recourse against each other, so they become completely different, and reconciliation is impossible because the recent cannot be imagined to be ancient, nor the ancient recent. The author made the claim of inheritance like the claim of purchase, while the well-known view is that it is like the claim of absolute ownership; so in Fath al-Qadir, and he asserted it in al-Bazzaziyya. He restricted it to a house to exclude debt because there is a difference regarding it. In Fath al-Qadir: If he claimed debt due to a loan, and witnesses testified to absolute ownership, it is not accepted. In al-Muhit it does not indicate acceptance, but in my view the opinion is acceptance because the indebtedness is for him, unlike real property. If he claimed against him a thousand as debt, and witnesses testified that he paid him a thousand, and we do not know by what method he paid, it is said it is not accepted, but the more correct view is that it is accepted; so in al-Bazzaziyya. The author omitted two conditions in the claim of purchase: first, that he claims it from a known person, like saying 'My property I bought from so-and-so' and mentions the conditions of identification. However, if he says 'My property I bought from a man' or 'from Muhammad', and the witnesses testified... (continued)...
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This is an expansion probe from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, explaining the ruling on when someone claims a house by inheritance or purchase, but witnesses testify to absolute ownership (i.e., that he has owned it from the start). Such testimony is rejected because it exceeds the claim: the claimant asserts recent acquisition, while absolute ownership implies ancient, original ownership. The consequences (like entitlement to increments, recourse of sellers) differ, making reconciliation impossible. The author treats inheritance like purchase, but the well-known view equates inheritance with absolute ownership. The restriction to a house ('dar') excludes debt, because for debt there is a difference of opinion: in Fath al-Qadir, testimony of absolute ownership for a debt claimed due to loan is also rejected; but the commentator (likely Ibn Nujaym) suggests acceptance because debt does not carry increments like real property. For a debt claim of a thousand dirhams, if witnesses testify to payment of a thousand without specifying the reason, the preferred view is acceptance. The author also omitted two conditions for purchase claims: (1) the seller must be known; if the claimant says 'bought from a man' or 'from Muhammad' (unspecified), and witnesses testify to absolute ownership, it is accepted because the claim of purchase from an unknown is effectively a null admission, so absolute ownership testimony is valid. The text discusses the reasoning of Muhammad al-Shaybani: the claimant admitted ownership to the seller, then claimed transfer to himself via purchase, but witnesses did not witness transfer, so testimony rejected. This explains why for an unknown seller, absolute testimony is accepted. The commentary also cites al-Ramli and al-Tataarkhaniyya regarding a scenario where half of a debt is for a specific cause; testimony for absolute half is accepted. The difference between 'ayn (real property) and dayn (debt) is that real property can have increments, so absolute ownership testimony implies more than the claim; debt has no increments, so no contradiction. Thus, the ruling is nuanced.

Key Terms
ملك مطلق (mulk muṭlaq): Absolute ownership that is not restricted to a particular cause (like purchase or inheritance); it is inherent or original ownership.
ملك حادث (mulk ḥādith): Recent or acquired ownership, such as through purchase or inheritance, which is temporally limited.
زوائد (zawā'id): Increments or accretions that accrue to property, such as fruits, offspring, or rent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is testimony of absolute ownership rejected when the claim is for purchase of a house?
A) Because the witnesses are not trustworthy.
B) Because absolute ownership implies ancient ownership, while purchase implies recent acquisition, making the testimony exceed the claim.
C) Because the house is not specified properly.
D) Because purchase requires a written contract.

Key Takeaway
Testimony must match the claim; absolute ownership and recent acquisition are legally distinct and cannot be reconciled, making absolute testimony invalid when a specific cause is claimed.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
8 min
Rejection of Testimony on Absolute Ownership When Claim is Specific
Ruling on when witnesses testify to absolute ownership while plaintiff claims specific cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when witnesses testify to absolute ownership while the plaintiff claims a specific cause, and explain the rationale based on the contradiction between old and new ownership.

Core Idea
Absolute ownership testimony is rejected when plaintiff claims new ownership; the two cannot coexist.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, Ibn Nujaym explains the principle that testimony must match the claim in both subject and cause. When witnesses testify to absolute ownership (ملك مطلق) while the plaintiff claims ownership by purchase or inheritance (ملك حادث), the testimony is void because it contradicts the claim. This lesson unpacks the rationale and the intra-Hanafi differences on this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and testimony
The plaintiff claims a house by inheritance or purchase; witnesses testify to absolute ownership without specifying the cause. This creates a mismatch between the claim and the testimony.
Absolute ownership is ownership without mention of how it was acquired.
2
Analyze the contradiction
Absolute ownership implies ownership from the origin (ملك قديم), entitling the owner to accessories (زوائد) like fruits and rents. New ownership (ملك حادث) is limited to the act of acquisition and does not include pre-existing accessories. These two concepts are fundamentally incompatible.
The new cannot be old, nor the old new.
3
State the Hanafi ruling
The testimony is rejected (تلغو) because the witnesses testified to more than what was claimed. They essentially gave the plaintiff more rights than he sought, creating a contradiction that invalidates the testimony.
The ruling is that the testimony is void (batil).
4
Explore intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad gives an alternative reasoning: the plaintiff, by claiming purchase, implicitly confessed ownership to the seller, and the witnesses did not corroborate the transfer, so the testimony is rejected. This explains why if the claim is from an unknown person, the testimony is accepted. Also, regarding inheritance, the well-known view treats inheritance as like absolute ownership, so testimony to absolute ownership would be accepted, but the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq treats it like purchase.
There is also a difference for debt (dyn) vs. chattel ('ayn).
5
Apply the distinction to debt
For debt, some Hanafi scholars accept testimony of absolute ownership because debt does not have accessories, so the contradiction does not arise. The text mentions that the correct view is acceptance for debt.
This exception is based on the nature of debt as a financial obligation.

Chapter of Testimonies, Section on Disagreement in Testimony. (Matn): 'If he claims a house by inheritance or purchase and two witnesses testify to absolute ownership, it is void.' Explanation: The evidence is not accepted because they testified to more than what the plaintiff claimed. He claimed a new ownership while they testified to an old ownership, and they are different. Absolute ownership is established from the origin so that the claimant is entitled to its accessories, unlike new ownership. And the sellers have recourse against each other, so they become two different things and reconciliation is impossible because the new cannot be old nor the old new. The author made the claim of inheritance like the claim of purchase, while the well-known view is that it is like the claim of absolute ownership; as in Fath al-Qadir, and it is affirmed in al-Bazzaziyya. He restricted it to a house to exclude debt because there is disagreement. In Fath al-Qadir: If he claims debt due to a loan and they testify to absolute ownership, it is not accepted. In al-Muhit: it does not indicate acceptance, but in my view the correct view is acceptance because the debt has priority for him unlike the chattel. And if he claims a thousand dirhams as a debt and they testify that he paid him a thousand but we don't know for what reason, it is said it is not accepted but the more correct is that it is accepted; as in al-Bazzaziyya. The author left two conditions in the claim of purchase: first, that he claims it from a known person... (continues similarly for the rest of the passage, covering conditions, differences between chattel and debt, effects of dating, etc.)
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling when a plaintiff claims a house by inheritance or purchase, but the witnesses testify to absolute ownership (without specifying the cause). The ruling is that the testimony is not accepted because they testified to more than what was claimed—they claimed old ownership while he claimed new ownership. This is a contradiction that cannot be reconciled. The author notes that the well-known view treats inheritance like purchase, but some say it is like absolute ownership. The passage then discusses exceptions: for debt, there is a difference of opinion; some accept testimony of absolute ownership for debt because debt does not have accessories. It also discusses conditions: the plaintiff must claim purchase from a known person, and he must not claim possession along with purchase. There is also discussion of historical dating, and cases where testimonies agree on the subject but differ in cause. The overall principle is that contradiction between claim and testimony leads to rejection, unless the contradiction is reconciled by a later agreement or by the fact that the witnesses testified to less than the claim. The passage also touches on the difference between chattels and debt, and the rationale based on the possibility of accessories.

Key Terms
ملك مطلق (mulk mutlaq): Absolute ownership without specification of cause, established from the origin
ملك حادث (mulk hadith): New ownership arising from a specific cause like purchase or inheritance
شهادة (shahadah): Testimony given in court by witnesses
زوائد (zawa'id): Accessories or increments (e.g., fruits, rents) that accrue to property

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a plaintiff claims a house by purchase, and two witnesses testify that the house is his absolute ownership, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The testimony is accepted and the plaintiff is entitled to the house.
B) The testimony is rejected because the witnesses testified to more than claimed.
C) The testimony is accepted only if the witnesses specify the purchase.
D) The testimony is rejected only if the plaintiff also claims possession.

Key Takeaway
Testimony must match the claim not only in the property but also in the cause of ownership; otherwise it is rejected.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate

Acceptance of Testimony When Claim and Witnesses Differ on Cause or Date
Discusses acceptance of testimony when claim and witnesses differ on ownership cause or date.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for accepting testimony when the claim and witnesses differ in specifying the cause or date of ownership, and apply these rules to various factual scenarios.

Core Idea
Testimony is accepted if it establishes less than what is claimed, but rejected if it introduces a different cause or contradicts the claimant's own statement.

In testimony, the goal is to prove the claim. When witnesses' statements deviate from the claim, the Hanafi school applies a nuanced set of rules based on whether the deviation is less, more, or different in cause. This lesson unpacks the principles from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq regarding absolute ownership, dating, and cause discrepancies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of discrepancy
Determine whether the difference between claim and testimony is in the specificity of ownership (absolute vs. specific), the date, or the cause of ownership.
This classification guides which rule applies.
2
Apply the principle of 'less' vs 'more'
If witnesses testify to something less than the claim (e.g., absolute ownership when claim specifies a cause), the testimony is accepted as it proves part of the claim. If they testify to more (e.g., dating when claimant didn't), it is also accepted in most cases, provided it is not contradictory.
The key is whether the testimony subsumes the claim or adds something consistent.
3
Check for cause mismatch
If the cause of ownership differs completely (e.g., claim based on sale, testimony based on usurpation), the testimony is rejected because the purpose differs. However, if the underlying purpose is the same (e.g., both are guarantees for the same amount), it may be accepted.
Difference in intended purpose is critical; if the purpose is the same, cause difference may be overlooked.
4
Consider the claimant's own words
If the claimant belies his witnesses by denying the confession they attest to, the testimony is rejected because the claimant's own statement contradicts the purpose.
The claimant cannot both rely on his witnesses and deny their account.
5
Note special cases
Bear in mind that ownership by gift is treated like ownership by purchase; if the claimant originally claimed absolute ownership but later specifies a cause, the testimony may be accepted. Also, if the claimant and witnesses repeat the claim and testimony in agreement after a discrepancy, it becomes accepted.
These exceptions demonstrate the school's flexibility.

كذا في البزازية) قال الرملي : والذي في البزازية : شهدا أنه أقر أنه كفل بألف عن زيد وقال الطالب نعم إنه أقر كذلك لكن كانت الكفالة عن خالد بها له أن يأخذ المال وتقبل الشهادة لإنفاقهما على المقصود فلا يضره اختلاف السبب، ولو قال الطالب لم يقر كذلك إلى آخر ما نقله هنا ففي النقل قصور كما ترى
From the Book of Testimonies, chapter on disagreement in testimony. Testimony on absolute ownership is accepted; thus in al-Khulasa. Second: that he does not claim possession with purchase; if he claims both, and they testify on absolute ownership, it is accepted; thus in al-Khulasa. It is restricted to cases where ownership has multiple causes, to exclude when ownership has a single cause: if they testify absolutely, it is accepted, as if he claims she is his wife due to marriage with such, and they testify that she is his wife without mentioning marriage, it is accepted and a dower of the like is awarded if equal to or less than the named amount, but if more than the named, the excess is not awarded, as in al-Khulasa. The author indicated that dated ownership is stronger than undated: so if he dates the claim in ownership and his witnesses are undated, it is not accepted; and in the opposite, the chosen view is acceptance, as in al-Khulasa. If he claims purchase and dates it, and they testify for him undated, it is accepted because it is less; and the reverse is not accepted. If there were two months for the purchase and they dated one month, it is accepted; and the reverse is not accepted; thus in Fath al-Qadir. And that if he claims by a cause and they testify by another cause, such as a thousand from price of a sale and they testify for a thousand from price of a usurped destroyed item, it is not accepted, as in al-Khulasa. This is if they differ in what is intended; if they agree on it, like a claim of a thousand as guaranty for so-and-so, and they testify for a thousand as guaranty for another, it is accepted, as in al-Khulasa also, unless the claimant says: he did not confess thus, but rather he confessed that it was a demandable guaranty, then it is not accepted because he has belied his witnesses; thus in al-Bazzaziyya. And similarly in causes of ownership of an item, as in al-Bazzaziyya also; he said: ownership by gift is like ownership by purchase, and likewise every contract is an event. So on this, if he claims an item due to purchase and they testify that it is his by gift, it is accepted. And in it also: if a discrepancy occurs between claim and testimony, then they repeat the claim and testimony and agree, it is accepted. And that if he bears testimony, it is accepted for the unknown also because they testified for more than he claims. His saying: (Second: that he does not claim possession with purchase, up to his saying: it is accepted) He said in Fath al-Qadir: a disagreement is reported in Fusul al-'Imadi; some said it is accepted because the claim of purchase with possession is a claim of absolute ownership, so no specification of the slave is required for the validity of this claim; some said no because the claim of purchase is considered in itself, not like the absolute. Do you not see that no award is made for the increments in that? His saying: (And if he claims purchase and dates it, etc.) is mentioned in al-Khulasa also. Consider the difference between it and what precedes; what appears to me is that testimony on absolute ownership without a date is stronger than it after he claims with a date, because without date it is possible primacy, so in the testimony there is an increase, so the derivation mentioned is not sound. Ponder. His saying: (because he belied his witnesses, thus in al-Bazzaziyya) Al-Ramli said: what is in al-Bazzaziyya: they testified that he confessed that he guaranteed a thousand for Zaid, and the claimant said: yes, he confessed thus, but the guaranty was for Khalid, so he has the right to take the money, and the testimony is accepted because they agreed on the purpose, so the difference of cause does not harm. And if the claimant said: he did not confess thus... to the end of what he quoted here; the quotation is deficient as you see.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the chapter of disagreements in testimony, specifically focusing on cases where the claim (da'wa) and the testimony (shahada) differ in specification. The commentator expands on the rulings of the matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq). The core principle is that if the witnesses testify to something less than what the claimant alleged, their testimony is accepted because it establishes a part of the claim. If they testify to something more, the testimony is rejected unless the claimant agrees. The passage first discusses the rule regarding absolute ownership (al-mulk al-mutlaq) versus ownership by a specific cause (sabab). If the claimant alleges a specific cause (e.g., purchase or marriage), but the witnesses testify to absolute ownership without mentioning the cause, the testimony is accepted provided the claimant did not also claim possession (qabd) along with the purchase. However, if ownership has only one possible cause (e.g., marriage), then absolute testimony suffices. The commentator notes a restriction: the rule applies when ownership can have multiple causes; if a single cause is possible, absolute testimony is accepted. Regarding dated claims, if the claimant dates his ownership and the witnesses give undated testimony, it is accepted because it is less; conversely, if the claimant does not date and the witnesses date, the testimony is also accepted (according to the chosen view) because it strengthens the claim. However, detailed conditions apply, e.g., if the claimant dates a purchase to a specific month and the witnesses date it to a different month, the testimony is accepted only if the witnesses' date is earlier (less than the claim). The passage then covers cases where the cause differs completely, such as claiming a debt from a sale and witnesses testifying for a debt from a usurped item: rejected because the cause is different and the claimant's purpose is not met. But if the purpose is the same (e.g., both involve a guarantee for the same amount, only the guarantor differs), the testimony is accepted unless the claimant explicitly belies his witnesses. The commentator also clarifies that ownership by gift is akin to ownership by purchase for these rules. Finally, he discusses the scenario of bearing testimony (tahammul al-shahada) for an unknown amount: if the claim is for an unspecified amount and the witnesses specify it, it is accepted because they testify to more than the claim. The commentary cites various sources such as al-Khulasa, Fath al-Qadir, and al-Bazzaziyya, and includes critical remarks from later scholars like al-Ramli.

Key Terms
الملك المطلق (al-mulk al-mutlaq): Absolute ownership without specifying the cause or date.
التوفيق (al-tawfiq): The claimant's agreement or reconciliation with the witnesses' testimony after a discrepancy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a claimant alleges he bought an item for 1000, and witnesses testify that the item is his without mentioning a cause, what is the ruling?
A) The testimony is rejected because it does not match the claim.
B) The testimony is accepted because it is less than the claim.
C) The testimony is accepted only if the claimant also claims possession.
D) The testimony is rejected because the cause is different.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi approach to testimony discrepancy revolves around the principle that the witness must establish the claim or a part thereof; additions or changes may require the claimant's ratification.

Testimony for Less or More: The Rule of 'Bi-'Aksihi' and the Explanation Condition
Explains the rule of reversed case (bi-'aksihi) and detailed scenarios of testimony for less and more.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when testimony for less or more than the claim is accepted in Hanafi law, and apply the condition that the lesser testimony must be capable of explaining the claim.

Core Idea
Testimony for less is accepted only if it can explain the claim; testimony for more requires claimant's agreement.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, the commentator expands on the rule of 'aks (reverse) – when a claimant states an absolute ownership and witnesses specify a cause. The key is understanding the degrees of specificity and how the court handles discrepancies between claim and testimony. This lesson will walk you through the core principles and the important exception regarding natāj (offspring) versus purchase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relationship: less or more?
Compare the claim and the testimony: does the testimony assert a subset (less) or a superset (more) of the claimed right? For example, claiming absolute ownership while testifying to a specific cause is less; claiming offspring while testifying to purchase is complicated because purchase is a different kind of cause.
The text defines a hierarchy: cause < absolute < offspring in terms of certainty and specificity.
2
Check if the lesser testimony can explain the claim (yashluḥ bayānan)
The condition for accepting testimony for less is that the lesser assertion can logically serve as an explanation of the greater claim. For example, testifying to purchase can explain an absolute ownership claim, but testifying to purchase cannot explain a claim of offspring (natāj) because offspring implies original birth, not acquisition.
If the testimony cannot explain the claim, it is rejected unless the claimant explicitly reconciles (e.g., by stating a sequence of events).
3
Apply the rule to the reverse case (bi-'aksihi)
When the claim is absolute and the testimony specifies a cause, the judge must ask the claimant whether his ownership is due to that cause or another. If he affirms, judgment is given; if he denies, no ruling is issued.
This is a procedural safeguard to ensure the testimony matches the actual basis of ownership.
4
Distinguish the two omitted cases: past ownership and insha' vs. iqrar
The author notes that if someone claims ownership for the present but witnesses testify it was owned in the past, the testimony is accepted based on istishab (presumption of continuity). Similarly, claiming a direct act (insha') while witnesses testify to a confession (iqrar) involves a different rule not fully expanded here.
The acceptance of past ownership testimony is controversial; some scholars argue that istishab only works for defense, not for establishing rights.
5
Evaluate the critique of istishab
The commentator criticizes the justification in Jami' al-Fusulayn that accepting past testimony is to 'remove hardship,' calling it a weak argument. This highlights that while the Hanafi school accepts the practice, the epistemological basis is debated.
The critique does not change the operative ruling but sharpens your understanding of the school's methodology.

His saying: and its reverse (i.e., if he claims absolute ownership and they testify by a specific cause, it is not void, so it is accepted because they testified to less than what he claimed, and that is not preclusive; he made it unrestricted. And he restricted it in al-Khulasa: that the judge asks: do you own it by this cause they testified to or another? If he says by this cause, he rules for ownership by this cause; if he says by another cause, no ruling is given at all. The upshot: ownership by cause is less than absolute ownership because it implies primacy, unlike cause which implies occurrence; and absolute is less than offspring (natāj) because absolute implies primacy by probability, and offspring by certainty. In al-Bazzaziyya: if he claims offspring and they testify for purchase, it is not accepted, unless the claimant agrees and says: it gave birth at my place, then I sold it to him, then I bought it back, then it is accepted; thus in al-Khaniyya. The upshot: when they testify to more than what he claimed, if the claimant agrees, it is accepted in all cases; otherwise not. This is something that should be memorized, and we have mentioned it from al-Khaniyya. The author did not mention two issues: firstly, if he claims something for the present and they testify for it in the past, and its reverse; secondly, if he claims a direct act (insha') and they testify for a confession (iqrar), or its reverse. As for the first: in al-Muhit, quoting from al-Aqdiyya and Adab al-Qadi of al-Khassaf: if he claims ownership for the present, i.e., in the item, and they testify that this item was owned by him in the past, it is accepted because they established ownership in the past, so judgment is given for the present unless something removing it is known. Rashid al-Din mentioned it as an issue and narrated that he... The matn: 'Whoever testifies and does not leave until he says: I became mistaken in part of my testimony, it is accepted if he was upright.' So it is conditioned on not leaving, and the apparent has preceded. His saying: (In al-Bazzaziyya: if he claims offspring and they testify for purchase, it is not accepted) It is not hidden that testimony for purchase is testimony for ownership by cause, which is less than offspring, so it is testimony for less. And it has passed that testimony for less than claimed is accepted without reconciliation. And it appears from the words of al-Khaniyya that testimony for less is accepted if that less can serve as an explanation of what he claimed. For he first mentioned that if he claims a house in someone's hand and that it is his, and they testify that he bought it from the possessor, it is valid because their testimony is for less than he claimed, and what they testified to can serve as an explanation of what the claimant claimed. For if he says: it is mine because I bought it from the possessor, it is valid, and his latter speech is an explanation of the former. Unlike if he first claimed offspring and they testified for purchase from the possessor, it is not accepted unless he reconciles; otherwise no. Because the claim of offspring against the possessor does not admit a claim of newly acquired ownership from him; for if he says: this animal is mine by offspring from the possessor, his speech is not valid. So it cannot be made that his latter speech is an explanation of the former, and the testimony is not accepted without reconciliation. So ponder. In Nur al-'Ayn: if he claims it as offspring and they testify for absolute, it is accepted, not the reverse, because the claim of absolute is a claim of primary ownership by probability, and testimony of offspring is primary ownership by certainty, so they testified to more than he claimed, so it is rejected. And this issue indicates that if he claims offspring then absolute, it is accepted, not the reverse. I say: he claimed offspring and they testified by a cause, it is rejected. His saying: (so judgment is given for the present, etc.) The author of Jami' al-Fusulayn said: this is acting on istishab (presumption of continuity), which is a proof for defense, not for entitlement. So it should have been that their testimony is not accepted, but there is hardship, so it is accepted to remove hardship. The humble one says: his statement 'to remove hardship' is a weak justification.

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the chapter of disagreements in testimony, focusing on the 'reverse case' (bi-'aksihi) – where the claimant alleges absolute ownership and witnesses specify a cause. The commentator explains that this is accepted because the witnesses testify to less than the claim. However, al-Khulasa restricts this: the judge must ask the claimant whether his ownership is due to the cause the witnesses specified or another; only if he answers positively does the judge rule by that cause. The passage then compares degrees of specificity: ownership by cause is less than absolute ownership because it establishes a specific time of origin (hadath), while absolute ownership implies primacy (ula) only by probability. The next topic is the case of 'offspring' (natāj) versus purchase. If a claimant claims an animal is his by offspring (i.e., it was born in his possession) and witnesses testify that he bought it, the testimony is rejected because it establishes a different kind of ownership; but if the claimant reconciles by explaining a sequence of events (e.g., it was born to him, then sold and repurchased), it is accepted. The commentator emphasizes a key rule: if witnesses testify to more than the claim, it is rejected unless the claimant agrees; if less, it is accepted without agreement, provided the lesser can serve as a valid explanation of the claim. The author of the matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) omitted two cases: (1) claiming ownership for the present and witnesses testifying to past ownership – the commentary, citing al-Muhit, says such testimony is accepted based on istishab (presumption of continuity), allowing judgment for the present unless a contrary is known. (2) Claiming a direct creation of right (insha') versus testimony of confession (iqrar) – the passage does not expand fully. Finally, a matn quote: if a witness says he made a mistake in part of his testimony before leaving the court, his testimony is accepted if he is upright. The commentator critiques a justification by Jami' al-Fusulayn that acceptance in the past-ownership case is to remove hardship, calling it a weak explanation.

Key Terms
النتاج (al-natāj): Ownership by offspring (e.g., a animal born in one's possession), which establishes primary ownership (ula) with certainty.
الاستصحاب (al-istishab): Presumption of continuity of a prior state; used to validate acceptance of testimony for past ownership to rule for present.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The testimony is accepted only if the claimant also claims possession.
D) The testimony is rejected unconditionally.

Key Takeaway
The acceptance of testimony depends on the relationship between claim and testimony: less is accepted if it can explain the claim; more requires the claimant's approval.

Testimony of Past Ownership for Present Claim
Discusses acceptance of testimony for past ownership when claiming present ownership, using istishab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for accepting testimony of past ownership to prove present ownership and apply the principle of istishab to determine when such testimony is sufficient.

Core Idea
Past ownership proves present ownership via istishab unless removal is known.

This lesson addresses a nuance in testimony law: if a claimant asserts current ownership of a property but the witnesses only testify that he owned it in the past, is that sufficient? The Hanafi school, drawing on the principle of istishab, answers yes for physical assets, as long as no evidence of transfer has appeared. Understanding this ruling helps you see how presumption of continuity operates in fiqh and how it balances evidentiary gaps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and testimony mismatch
Note that the claimant asserts present ownership (al-milk li'l-hal), but the witnesses only confirm past ownership (milk fi al-madi). This mismatch is the core issue.
2
Recognize the Hanafi method: istishab
The Hanafi position relies on istishab—the presumption that a state (here, ownership) continues unless there is evidence of change. Since past ownership is established, present ownership is inferred.
Istishab is a secondary proof in Hanafi usul, used here to bridge the testimonial gap.
3
Confirm the asset type
The ruling specifically applies to physical assets ('ayn), not debts or mere possession (yad). For debts, the mufta bihi position is that such testimony is not accepted (as will be discussed separately).
The passage explicitly mentions 'fi al-'ayn' (in the specific asset).
4
Note the exception
The acceptance holds 'as long as nothing is known to have removed it' (ma lam yu'lam al-muzil). If evidence of transfer or loss appears, the testimony ceases to be sufficient.
This exception preserves the principle that istishab yields to contrary evidence.
5
Distinguish the related issue
The author also mentions a second issue (testimony on insha' vs iqrar) which is left for another lesson. Focus on the first ruling for now.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the author did not mention two issues: one is when someone claims something for the present and they testify about it in the past, and its opposite; the second is when he claims establishment (insha') and they testify by acknowledgment (iqrar), or its opposite. As for the first, in al-Muhit, quoting from al-Aqdiya and Adab al-Qadi by al-Khassaf: If someone claims ownership for the present, i.e., in a specific asset, and they testify that this asset was previously owned by him, it is accepted because they established ownership in the past, so judgment is given for the present as long as nothing is known to have removed it. Rashid al-Din said after mentioning it: 'An order, and it is narrated that he...'

Commentary
This passage identifies two issues not covered by the author (Ibn Nujaym) in the main text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The first issue: a claimant asserts that he currently owns a property, but the witnesses only testify that he owned it in the past. The Hanafi position, quoted from al-Muhit, is that such testimony is accepted because it establishes past ownership, and by the principle of istishab (presumption of continuity), the ownership is deemed to continue in the present unless evidence of transfer is known. This is for physical assets ('ayn). The second issue is mentioned but not elaborated here. The passage also cites Rashid al-Din's view.

Key Terms
استصحاب (istishab): Presumption of continuity of a state or ownership until evidence of change appears.
المالك للحال (al-milk li'l-hal): Current/present ownership of a property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Rejected because they did not testify about the present.
B) Accepted only if the plaintiff says he still owns it.
C) Accepted because past ownership implies present ownership by istishab.
D) Rejected because testimony must be about the present.

Key Takeaway
Testimony of past ownership for a physical asset is accepted to prove present ownership via istishab, unless evidence of removal exists.

Immediate Witness Retraction and Testimony for Past Debt
Matn ruling and detailed talil on past debt testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a witness who retracts immediately before leaving the court, and explain why testimony of past debt is not accepted to prove a current claim, distinguishing it from testimony of past ownership of physical property.

Core Idea
A witness who retracts before leaving court is still credible; but past debt testimony does not prove present debt.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents two interconnected rulings on witness testimony. First, the matn gives a surprising leniency: if a witness immediately corrects himself before leaving the court, his testimony remains valid if he is just. Then, a lengthy discussion explores whether testimony that a debt existed in the past suffices to prove the debt now. The Hanafi school firmly distinguishes between debts and physical property, rejecting such testimony for debts while accepting it for physical assets through the principle of istishab. We will unpack the reasoning and the intra-Hanafi dissent from al-'Imadi.

Guided Path
1
State the matn ruling on immediate retraction
Identify the exact condition: the witness must not have left the court (lam yabrah) and must say 'awhimtu' (I made a mistake). The ruling: the testimony is accepted if he is just ('adl).
This is the plain text of the matn; the commentary expands on it.
2
Identify the dispute on testimony for past debt
The core issue: if a witness testifies that the defendant owed a debt in the past, does that prove the debt exists now? The relied-upon Hanafi position says no; the witness must affirm the debt is still due or that the debtor died while indebted.
This contrasts with physical property ('ayn), where testimony of past ownership is accepted via istishab.
3
Analyze the opposing view (al-'Imadi)
Some Hanafi scholars, like al-'Imadi, argued by qiyas that past debt testimony should be accepted just like past property testimony, because the same presumption of continuity (istishab) should apply. The text rebuts this by citing explicit nusus (transmitted rulings) that reject it for debts.
This is a legitimate intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, but the mufta bihi is the majority view.
4
Distinguish between 'ayn and dayn in testimony
Physical property: testimony of past ownership suffices because ownership persists until proven otherwise. Debt: the ruling is stricter; mere past indebtedness does not guarantee present debt, as debts are more susceptible to discharge without the claimant's knowledge.
The text from Fath al-Qadir illustrates: if a witness says 'I don't know if it's still owed now', the testimony is rejected.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical case
Consider a creditor claiming a debt from a living debtor, with witnesses who only recall the original loan but are uncertain if it was repaid. According to the final fatwa, the claim fails. But if the debtor is deceased, the witnesses must testify that he died while still indebted.
This shows the thamarah al-khilaf: the practical difference between accepted and rejected testimony.

جليل ؛ كذا في نور العين قوله : ومعنى هذا لا يحل للقاضي أن يقول الخ) قال الرملي : أي لأنهم لو قالوا لا نعلم أنه ملكه اليوم لا تقبل شهادتهم فيضيع حق المدعي ظاهراً فلا يسألهم بخلاف ما إذا قال لهم هل تعلمون أنه خرج عن ملكه فإنهم إذا قالوا لا نعلم أنه خرج عن ملكه لا تبطل شهادتهم كما هو ظاهر قوله : (ينبغي أن تقبل الخ) قال الرملي : مع خلافه وسيأتي أن بحثه لا يعارض المنصوص إذ لا عبرة للأبحاث في مقابلة The matn: 'Whoever testifies and does not leave until he says: "I made a mistake in part of my testimony," it is accepted if he is just.' So he restricted it to not leaving, and the apparent is... His statement: 'In al-Bazzaziyya: If he claims progeny and they testify for purchase, it is not accepted.' It is clear that testimony for purchase is testimony for ownership by cause, which is less than progeny, so it is testimony for less. And it has been mentioned that testimony for less than claimed is accepted without agreement. And it appears from the words of al-Khaniyya that testimony for less is accepted if that less can serve as an explanation of what he claimed... [continues] ... The conclusion is that the transmitted ruling regarding physical property is as you heard; as for debt, the transmitted ruling is non-acceptance. He said in Fath al-Qadir: They testified about a man's admission of a debt, and the one against whom the claim is made said: 'Do you testify that it is now?' and he replied: 'I do not know if it is still owed to you now or not' – the testimony is not accepted. And before that: If someone claims a debt against a deceased, and they testify that he had a debt against the deceased, it is not accepted until they testify that he died while still owing it... [continues]
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage contains multiple rulings. First, the matn ruling: a witness who immediately retracts part of his testimony before leaving the court is still considered credible if he is just ('adl). The commentary then returns to the earlier discussion of testifying for cause versus progeny, elaborating on when testimony for less is accepted as an explanation of the claim. It criticizes the reasoning of Jam' al-Fusulayn that accepting testimony of past ownership is based on istishab, which is for defense not entitlement, but accepts it to avoid hardship. The author also discusses the view of al-'Imadi that testimony of past debt should be accepted by analogy to physical property, but the relied-upon view in the madhhab is that it is not accepted. The text clearly distinguishes between 'ayn (physical property) and dayn (debt), and also between testimony of ownership and testimony of possession (yad). It cites Fath al-Qadir and al-Bazzaziyya, and concludes with a note about debt of a deceased, requiring testimony that the deceased died while owing it. The passage also includes a quote from al-Ramli commenting on the judge's questioning.

Key Terms
لم يبرح (lam yabrah): The witness has not left the place of testimony/court session.
أوهِم (awhimtu): I made a mistake or erred, possibly due to forgetfulness (wahm).
شهادة على إقرار (shahada 'ala iqrar): Testimony concerning an admission or confession made by a party.
دين ميت (dayn mayyit): A debt owed by a deceased person.
استصحاب (istishab): Presumption of continuity of a state until proven otherwise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The testimony is rejected completely.
B) The testimony is accepted because he is just.
C) The witness is disqualified for lying.
D) The judge must investigate the mistake.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a witness's immediate retraction before leaving court does not invalidate testimony, but testimony of past debt never proves a current claim unless the debt's persistence is affirmed.

Acceptance of Testimony about Past Ownership and the Judge's Questioning
Ruling expansion on past testimony and judge's questioning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on testimony about past ownership and explain the correct form of judicial questioning regarding property ownership.

Core Idea
Past ownership testimony is accepted; judge must ask about exit from ownership, not current state.

How should a judge question witnesses about property ownership when they only know the past? This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that the judge should not ask about the present state, which witnesses may not know with certainty. Instead, the question must focus on whether the claimant has exited from ownership. The passage then expands on related rulings for debts and marriage, and distinguishes between testimony about ownership versus mere possession. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the Hanafi reliance on the presumption of continuity (istishab) in testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling on past ownership testimony
Recognise that the passage states testimony about past ownership is accepted. The judge should not ask about current ownership but about exit from ownership.
This is based on istishab: the presumption that past ownership continues until proven otherwise.
2
Extend the ruling to debts and marriage
Note that Al-Imadi analogizes from property (ayn) to debt and marriage: testimony that a debt existed or that a marriage occurred in the past is accepted without needing to address the present.
This shows the principle applies broadly to legal relationships.
3
Distinguish between ownership and possession testimony
Contrast the acceptance of past ownership testimony with the rejection of past possession (yad) testimony in the apparent narration. Abu Yusuf dissents.
Possession alone is weaker proof of ownership.
4
Analyse the refutation of Al-Imadi's deduction
Understand why the commentator refutes Al-Imadi's conclusion from al-Waqi'at, showing that the case of payment testimony does not automatically validate past debt testimony.
The distinction is between testimony permitted to be given and testimony that is accepted.
5
Apply the ruling using istishab
From al-Bazzaziyya: even if witnesses do not know the present state, judgment is given by istishab, because the witness to the contract is considered a witness to the present.
Istishab bridges the gap between past testimony and present ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشهادات باب الاختلاف في الشهادة سيد أنت ا هـ. ومعنى هذا لا يحل للقاضي أن يقول أتعلمون أنه ملكه اليوم نعم ينبغي للقاضي أن يقول هل تعلمون أنه خرج عن ملكه فقط؛ ذكره في المحيط . قال العمادي : فعلى هذا لو ادعى الدين فشهدوا أنه كان له عليه كذا ينبغي أن تقبل كما في العين، ومثله ما لو ادعى أنها زوجته فشهدوا أنه كان تزوجها ولم يتعرضوا للحال تقبل. هذا كله إذا شهدوا بالملك في الماضي، أما لو شهدوا باليد له في الماضي لا يقضى به في ظاهر الرواية وإن كانت اليد تسوغ الشهادة في الملك على ما أسلفناه، وعن أبي يوسف يقضى بها. وخرج العمادي على هذا ما في الواقعات لو أقر بدين عند رجلين ثم شهد عدلان عند الشاهد أنه قضى دينه أن شاهدي الإقرار يشهدان أنه كان له عليه دين ولا يشهدان أن له عليه فقال هذا أيضاً دليل على أنه إذ ادعى العين وشهدوا أنه كان له عليه تقبل وهذا غلط فإنه إنما تعرض لما يسوغ له أن يشهد به لا للقبول وعدمه بل ربما يؤخذ من منعه من إحدى العبارتين دون الأخرى ثبوت القبول في إحداهما دون الأخرى، كيف وقد ثبت بشهادة العدلين عند الشاهدين أنه قضاه فلا يشهدان حتى بخبر القاضي بذلك وأن القاضي حينئذ لا يقضي بشيء؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وفي البزازية : شهدا أنها زوجت نفسها ولا نعلم أنها في الحال امرأته أو لا أو شهدوا أنه باع منه هذا العين ولا ندري أنه ملكه في الحال أم لا يقضي بالنكاح والملك في الحال بالاستصحاب والشاهد في العقد شاهد في الحال ا هـ.
Book of Testimonies, Chapter on Conflict in Testimony. ... And the meaning of this is that it is not permissible for the judge to say: 'Do you know that it is his property today?' Rather, the judge should say: 'Do you know that he has exited from his ownership only?' This is mentioned in al-Muhit. Al-Imadi said: On this basis, if he claims a debt and they testify that he had such and such upon him, it should be accepted, just as in the case of property. Similarly, if he claims that she is his wife and they testify that he had married her without addressing the present state, it is accepted. This all applies if they testify to ownership in the past. As for if they testify to possession (yad) for him in the past, it is not judged by it in the apparent narration, even though possession permits testimony in ownership as we have mentioned. And from Abu Yusuf, it is judged by it. Al-Imadi derived from this what is in al-Waqi'at: If he confesses to a debt in front of two men, then two upright witnesses testify in front of the witness that he paid his debt, the two witnesses of the confession testify that he had a debt upon him, and they do not testify that it is upon him now. He said: This also indicates that if he claims a property and they testify that he had it, it is accepted. But this is an error, because he only addressed what permits him to testify, not acceptance or non-acceptance. Rather, it may be inferred from his prohibition of one expression over another that acceptance is proven in one over the other. How so, when it has been established by the testimony of the two upright witnesses in front of the two witnesses that he paid it, so they do not testify until the judge informs them of that, and then the judge does not decide anything? This is in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Bazzaziyya: Two witnesses testify that she married herself, and we do not know whether she is his wife at present or not, or they testify that he sold this property and we do not know whether he owned it at present or not — judgment is given for marriage and ownership at present by istishab (presumption of continuity), and the witness to the contract is a witness to the present. End quote.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the rules of testimony when witnesses speak about a past state of ownership or legal relationship. The passage type is a ruling expansion: the commentator explains and expands upon the base text (Kanz al-Daqa'iq). The central issue: if witnesses testify that someone owned a property or owed a debt in the past, but do not address the present, is their testimony accepted? The passage first clarifies that a judge should not ask witnesses directly whether the property still belongs to the claimant today, because that implies they must know the current state with certainty. Instead, the judge should ask whether they know that the claimant has exited from ownership — if they answer no, their testimony remains valid by presumption of continuity (istishab). Al-Imadi extends this to debts: if witnesses say 'he had such on him' (past tense) for a living debtor, it should be accepted, analogizing to property (ayn). Similarly, for marriage: witnesses saying 'he married her' without mentioning present state is accepted. Then the passage distinguishes testimony about ownership vs. mere possession (yad): if witnesses only testify to past possession, the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) says it is not accepted for establishing ownership, though Abu Yusuf differs and accepts it. The passage then refutes Al-Imadi's deduction from a case in al-Waqi'at, where witnesses to a confession of debt later testified to its payment — the commentator argues that this case actually shows that when witnesses testify only to past debt without present, it is not necessarily accepted. Finally, from al-Bazzaziyya, a ruling is given: if witnesses testify to a marriage contract or sale without knowing current status, judgment is given by istishab (presumption of continuity), because the witness to the contract is considered a witness to the present. This shows the Hanafi reliance on istishab to validate past testimony when the current state is unknown.

Key Terms
الاستصحاب (al-istishab): Presumption of continuity: a legal presumption that a state existing in the past continues until evidence of change.
اليد (al-yad): Possession or control over property; here it refers to mere physical possession as opposed to full ownership (milk).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how should a judge question witnesses about property ownership?
A) Ask: 'Do you know it is his property today?'
B) Ask: 'Do you know he has exited from his ownership?'
C) Ask: 'Is this property in his possession?'
D) Ask: 'Was this property his before?'

Key Takeaway
Testimony about past ownership is accepted in Hanafi fiqh, and the judge must ask about exit from ownership, relying on istishab to bridge the past to the present.
