Testimony for Past Debts: Living vs Deceased and the Role of Istishab
Explains rulings on testimony for past debts and the role of istishab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on accepting testimony for past debts on living and deceased persons, and explain why istishab is not a sufficient basis for witnesses.

Core Idea
Testimony for a past debt on a deceased requires stating he died owing; for a living person it is accepted unless the opponent challenges the present.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq, the author distinguishes between testimony about a debt on a living person versus a deceased person. The key factor is the certainty of the witness: the owner can rely on istishab (presumption of continuity), but a witness cannot. The passage also discusses the effect of past-tense wording in ownership claims, showing how careful the Hanafi school is about linguistic precision in testimony.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two cases
Identify the two scenarios: testimony about a debt on a living person and on a deceased person. Note the different rulings.
The living debtor case is more lenient; the deceased case is stricter.
2
Identify the explicit ruling for each
For a living debtor, past-tense testimony is accepted unless the opponent asks about its present state and the witnesses say they don't know. For a deceased debtor, such testimony is never accepted unless the witnesses explicitly state that he died while the debt was upon him.
The ruling is from Fath al-Qadir and is considered zahir al-riwayah.
3
Understand the reasoning: istishab and certainty
The witness cannot rely on istishab to affirm present ownership because he lacks certainty about its continuation. The owner, however, has certain knowledge of his ownership and can rely on istishab. Therefore, the deceased's estate requires an explicit statement as a precaution.
This is a key Hanafi principle: testimony requires certainty, not mere presumption.
4
Analyze the opposite scenario: past ownership claims
When a claimant uses past tense ('this was mine') and witnesses testify in present tense, there is disagreement; the soundest view is rejection because the claimant's wording negates present claim. If both use past tense, it is also rejected because the claimant's aim is not to assert present ownership.
This highlights the importance of isnaad (attribution) in testimony.
5
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-Imadi argued that past debt testimony on a living person should be accepted unconditionally, but the explicit ruling limits it. This shows ikhtilaf within the school and the tarjih mechanism.
The later authority (Fath al-Qadir, al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) upholds the explicit ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن المنصوص عليه في العين ما سمعت، وأما في الدين فالمنصوص عليه عدم القبول. قال في فتح القدير : شهدا على إقرار رجل بدين فقال المشهود عليه أتشهد أن هذا الآن فقال لا أدري أهو عليك الآن أم لا لا تقبل الشهادة ا هـ . وقال قبله : ادعى على آخر ديناً على مورثه وشهدوا أنه كان له على الميت دين لا تقبل حتى يشهدا أنه مات وهو عليه ا هـ . فموضوع الأولى في الشهادة على الإقرار وأن الشاهد قال لا أدري أهو عليك الآن أم لا وهو ساكت عما إذا شهدوا أنه كان له عليه كذا، وقد بحث العمادي أنه ينبغي القبول وليس بمعارض للمنصوص عليه كما علمت، وفي مسألة دين الميت لا بد في القبول من أن المنصوص لا يخفى على ذي فهم جليل؛ كذا في نور العين. اهـ. شهادتهما بأنه مات وهو عليه احتياطاً في أمر الميت ولهذا يحلف المدعي مع إقامة البيئة بخلافه في دين الحي فتحرر أنهما إذا شهدا في دين الحي بأنه كان له عليه كذا تقبل إلا إذا سألهما الخصم عن البقاء فقالا لا ندري وفي دين الميت لا تقبل مطلقاً. وأما عكسه فقال في جامع الفصولين: ولو ادعى ملكاً في الماضي وشهد به في الحال بأن قال كان هذا ملكي وشهدا أنه له قيل تقبل، وقيل لا تقبل وهو الأصح وكذا لو ادعى أنه كان له وشهدا أنه كان له لا تقبل لأن إسناذ المدعي يدل على نفي الملك في الحال إذ لا فائدة للمدعي في الإسناد مع قيام ملكه في الحال بخلاف الشاهدين لو أسندا ملكه إلى الماضي لأن إسنادهما لا يدل على النفي في الحال لأنهما لا يعرفان بقاءه إلا بالاستصحاب، والشاهد قد يحترز عن الشهادة بما ثبت باستصحاب الحال لعدم تيقنه بخلاف المالك إذ كما يعلم ثبوت ملكه يقيناً يعلم بقاءه يقيناً.
The conclusion: The explicit ruling regarding property is what you have heard. As for debt, the explicit ruling is non-acceptance. In Fath al-Qadir: Two witnesses testified to a man's confession of debt, and the defendant said: 'Do you testify that this is now?' He said: 'I do not know whether it is upon you now or not.' The testimony is not accepted. He also said earlier: If someone claims a debt against another from his testator, and witnesses testify that he had a debt on the deceased, it is not accepted until they testify that he died while it was upon him. So the subject of the first is testimony on confession, and the witness said 'I do not know if it is upon you now' — it is silent about when they testify that he had such upon him. Al-Imadi argued that it should be accepted, and it does not contradict the explicit ruling as you know. In the matter of the deceased's debt, acceptance requires that the explicit ruling is not hidden from one of great understanding. This is in Nur al-Ayn. End quote. Their testimony that he died while it was upon him is precautionary regarding the deceased. For this reason, the claimant takes an oath despite establishing evidence, unlike the case of a living person's debt. Thus it is established: if they testify in a living person's debt that he had such upon him, it is accepted, except if the opponent asks them about its remaining and they say 'we do not know'. In the case of a deceased person's debt, it is not accepted absolutely. As for its opposite, in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If he claims ownership in the past and testifies to it in the present by saying 'This was my property' and they testify that it is his, it is said to be accepted, and it is said it is not accepted, which is the soundest. Similarly, if he claims that it was his and they testify that it was his, it is not accepted, because the claimant's ascription indicates negation of ownership in the present, since there is no benefit for the claimant in ascribing it to the past while his ownership remains in the present — unlike the two witnesses, because their ascription does not indicate negation in the present, as they do not know its remaining except by istishab. The witness may avoid testimony based on istishab due to lack of certainty, unlike the owner, for just as he knows the establishment of his ownership with certainty, he knows its remaining with certainty.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on testimony about past debt, now distinguishing between a living debtor and a deceased one. The passage type is ruling with proof, as it cites explicit rulings from Fath al-Qadir and discusses the basis. The key point: for a living debtor, testimony that 'he had such on him' (past) is accepted unless the opponent specifically asks about present state and witnesses say they do not know. For a deceased debtor, such testimony is not accepted unless witnesses explicitly state that he died while owing. The reason is precaution for the deceased's estate: relying on istishab is considered insufficient because the witness may not have certainty of continuation. Additionally, the opposite scenario is discussed: if the claimant says 'this was my property' (past) and witnesses testify that it is his now (present), there is a difference of opinion; the soundest view is that it is not accepted because the claimant's own statement implies he does not claim present ownership, so the witnesses contradict him. If both claimant and witnesses use past tense, it is not accepted because the claimant's aim is not to claim present ownership (as he could if he owned it now). This highlights the Hanafi principle that testimony must be consistent and certain, and istishab has limits: a witness cannot rely on istishab to assert present state, but an owner can because his knowledge is certain. The passage also notes that Al-Imadi's view that past debt testimony should be accepted for living debtors does not contradict the explicit ruling in Fath al-Qadir, as that ruling only applies when witnesses explicitly say 'I don't know now'.

Key Terms
الإسناد (al-isnad): Attributing ownership to a specific time (past or present) in the claimant's statement or witnesses' testimony.
الاستصحاب (al-istishab): Presumption of continuity: here used to argue that if something was proven in the past, it continues until proven otherwise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If witnesses testify that a deceased person owed a debt but do not say 'he died while owing', is their testimony accepted?
A) Yes, because the debt existed in the past and by default continues.
B) No, because it is not accepted absolutely for a deceased debtor.
C) Yes, as long as the claimant takes an oath.
D) No, but it can be accepted if the judge uses istishab.

Key Takeaway
Testimony for a past debt requires specific wording depending on whether the debtor is living or deceased, reflecting the Hanafi emphasis on witness certainty.

Testimony on Confession: When the Cause Must Be Specified
Examining acceptance of testimony to confession without cause specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which contracts allow testimony to a confession without specifying the cause, and identify the exception for salam debt.

Core Idea
Testimony to a confession is accepted for most contracts, but for salam debt the cause must be specified because the debt type changes the ruling.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq compiles rulings on when testimony to a confession (iqrar) is accepted without mentioning the underlying cause. The general rule is broad acceptance for deposits, loans, marriage, and debts. However, an important exception emerges for salam (forward sale): if witnesses omit the cause 'salam', their testimony is not accepted because the legal characteristics of salam debt differ from ordinary debts. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The passage begins by listing contracts where testimony to a confession is accepted even if the witness does not mention the cause or current status: deposit (wadi'ah), usurpation (ghasb), loan for use (ariyah), marriage (nikah), and general debts (dayn). Establish that for these, the cause is irrelevant.
The rule is derived from Jami' al-Fusulayn: 'it is accepted as in usurpation.'
2
Spot the Exception: Salam
Next, the passage focuses on a salam claim: the claimant says 100 qafiz due to a valid salam contract. Witnesses testify only that the defendant confessed to owing 100 qafiz, without mentioning salam. Two views: (a) accepted because the difference is in cause only; (b) not accepted (sounder) because the debt differs by cause.
The key term: 'al-salam' – a forward sale with immediate payment and deferred delivery.
3
Understand the Reason for Non-Acceptance
The sounder view argues that salam debt and ordinary debts have different legal effects. For example, substitution (istibdal) before possession is prohibited in salam but permitted in ordinary debts. Therefore, the witnesses have not testified to the specific debt claimed, so the testimony is rejected.
Istibdal: replacing the debt item with something else before taking possession.
4
Distinguish from Loan (Qard)
Contrast with a loan (qard) claim: if the claimant alleges a loan and witnesses testify to a confession of a debt without specifying the cause, it is accepted. This is because the legal effects of a loan debt do not vary with cause – all money debts are fungible.
Qard is a simple loan of fungible items; salam is a specific type of sale.
5
Consider the Deceased Debtor Issue (Supplementary)
The passage ends with a separate dispute: for a deceased debtor, must witnesses explicitly say 'he died while owing'? The view from Jami' al-Fusulayn requires it, but Mu'in al-Hukkam holds that mere statement of the cause suffices. This reflects tension between caution for the deceased and protecting creditors' rights.
This is a secondary issue; the main lesson focuses on the salam exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثانية أعني ما إذا ادعى الإنشاء فشهدا بالإقرار أو عكسه فقال في جامع الفصولين : ادعى الوديعة وشهدا أن المودع أقر بالإيداع تقبل كما في الغصب، وكذا العارية ادعى نكاحاً وشهدا بإقرارهما بنكاح تقبل كما في الغصب ولو ادعى ديناً فشهدا بإقراره بالمال تقبل وتكون إقامة البينة على إقراره كإقامة البينة على السبب، وأفتى بعضهم بعدم القبول . ادعى قرضاً وشهدا بإقراره بالمال تقبل بلا بيان السبب ا هـ . فتقبل في الإيداع والغصب والعارية والديون والنكاح وأما البيع فقال في جامع الفصولين: ادعى بيعاً وشهدا أنه أقر بالبيع واختلفا في زمان ومكان تقبل وفيه قبله ادعى مائة قفيز بر بسبب سلم صحيح وشهدا أن المدعى عليه أقر أن له عليه مائة قفيز ولم يزيدا قيل تقبل لأنه اختلاف في سبب الدين فلا يمنع وقيل لا وهو الأصح لأنهما لم يذكرا إقراره بسبب السلم. والاختلاف في سبب الدين إنما يمنع قبولها لو لم يختلف الدين باختلاف السبب ودين السلم مع دين آخر يختلفان إذ الاستبدال قبل القبض لم يجز في السلم وجاز في دين البر بلا سبب فلم يشهدا بدين يدعيه فلا تقبل بخلاف ما لو ادعى بسبب القرض وشهدا أنه أقر ولم يذكرا بسبب القرض تقبل ا هـ . ثم قال : ادعى قضاء دينه وشهدا أنه أقر باستيفائه تقبل ا وفي القنية : رأيت بخط بعض العلماء وأقول : ما في المحيط لا يعارض ما في القنية إذ ما فيها فيما إذا ادعى الدين للحال فشهدا به كذلك بحيث أنهما لم يقولا كان وبه يحصل التوفيق فتأمل. وفي شرح تنوير الأبصار بعد نقل ما في البحر قال قلت ويعارض هذا ما في معين الحكام من قوله نقل عن المحيط أنه يثبت الدين على الميت بمجرد بيان الشاهدين سببه من غير أن يقولا مات وعليه دين اهـ. ونقل قوي ما في معين الحكام وأنه قال : إن الأول ضعيف وإن الاحتياط عن المقدسي بعض الفضائل في أمر الميت يكفي تحليف خصمه مع وجود بيئة وإن في هذا الاحتياط ترك احتياط آخر في وفاء دينه الذي يحجبه عن الجنة وتضييع حقوق أناس كثيرين لا يجدون من يشهد لهم على هذا الوجه اهـ. و به اعترض في نور العين على صاحب جامع الفصولين.
As for the second [case], namely when he claims a creation (insha') and they testify to a confession (iqrar) or its opposite, he said in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If he claims a deposit and they testify that the depositor confessed to the deposit, it is accepted, as in usurpation. Similarly, a loan for use (ariyah). If he claims a marriage and they testify to their confession of marriage, it is accepted, as in usurpation. If he claims a debt and they testify to his confession of property, it is accepted, and establishing evidence of his confession is like establishing evidence of the cause. Some gave a fatwa of non-acceptance. If he claims a loan (qard) and they testify to his confession of property, it is accepted without stating the cause — end quote. So it is accepted for deposit, usurpation, loan for use, debts, and marriage. As for sale, he said in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If he claims a sale and they testify that he confessed to the sale, and they differ in time and place, it is accepted. Earlier in it: If he claims one hundred qafiz of wheat due to a valid salam contract, and they testify that the defendant confessed that he owes him one hundred qafiz without adding more, it is said that it is accepted because the difference is in the cause of the debt and does not prevent. And it is said that it is not accepted, which is the sounder, because they did not mention his confession by the cause of salam. The difference in the cause of debt only prevents acceptance if the debt differs due to difference of cause. The salam debt and another debt differ, since substitution before possession is not permitted in salam, while it is permitted in unsecured wheat debt. So they have not testified to the debt that he claims, so it is not accepted. Unlike if he claims by cause of a loan and they testify that he confessed without mentioning the cause of the loan, it is accepted — end quote. Then he said: If he claims repayment of his debt and they testify that he confessed to receiving payment, it is accepted. In al-Qunyah: I saw in the handwriting of some scholars: I say: What is in al-Muhit does not contradict what is in al-Qunyah, because what is in it is for when he claims the debt at present and they testify accordingly such that they did not say 'was' — and by this reconciliation is achieved, so reflect. In Sharh Tanwir al-Absar after quoting from al-Bahr, he said: I say, this is contradicted by what is in Mu'in al-Hukkam, where it is quoted from al-Muhit that the debt is established on the deceased merely by the witnesses stating its cause without them saying 'he died with a debt'. Narration strong from Mu'in al-Hukkam, and he said: The first is weak, and the precaution regarding the deceased, according to some scholars, is that swearing his opponent with the presence of evidence is sufficient. And in this precaution is the abandonment of another precaution regarding fulfilling his debt which prevents him from Paradise, and wasting the rights of many people who cannot find witnesses on this basis. End quote. Nur al-Ayn objected to this against the author of Jami' al-Fusulayn.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the second category of cases: when the claimant bases his claim on a creation (like a contract) and witnesses testify to a confession (iqrar) by the defendant regarding that contract, or vice versa. The passage type is a compilation of rulings from Jami' al-Fusulayn and other sources. The general rule: testimony to a confession is accepted for deposit, usurpation, loan for use, marriage, and general debt — even if the witnesses do not specify the present state, because confession itself is a strong proof. However, for sale, a difference arises: if witnesses testify to a confession of sale but differ on time and place, it is still accepted; but if they testify to a confession of a debt of one hundred qafiz without specifying the cause (salam), it is not accepted (according to the sounder view) because the debt differs by cause — salam debt is different from an unsecured debt. On the other hand, if the claim is for a loan (qard), testimony to a confession without cause is accepted. A further issue: testimony that the debtor confessed to repayment (qada) is accepted. Then the passage discusses a potential contradiction between al-Muhit and al-Qunyah, and between Mu'in al-Hukkam and Jami' al-Fusulayn regarding the deceased debtor: some argue that mere statement of cause suffices for a deceased debtor, while the preferred view (as in Jami' al-Fusulayn) requires mentioning that he died while owing. The commentary in Sharh Tanwir al-Absar and Nur al-Ayn note this disagreement, with some preferring the view that establishes the debt more easily to protect the rights of creditors. This shows the practical tension between caution for the deceased and protecting creditors' rights.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrar): A confession or admission by the defendant; here it is the subject of testimony without the witness mentioning the underlying cause.
السلم (al-salam): A forward sale (salam contract) where payment is immediate but delivery is deferred; the debt nature differs from other debts.
الاستبدال (al-istibdal): Substitution or replacement of the debt item with another before taking possession. Prohibited in salam, permitted in ordinary debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which contract type requires witnesses to mention the cause (e.g., salam) in their testimony to a confession?
A) Deposit
B) Loan (qard)
C) Salam
D) Marriage

Key Takeaway
For most contracts, testimony to a confession is accepted without cause specification; for salam, the cause must be mentioned due to the unique legal nature of salam debt.

Combining Testimony on Ownership and Admission for Slaves
Ruling on combining testimony of ownership and admission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting testimony when one witness testifies to absolute ownership and another to admission, and explain why the ruling differs for slaves versus land/female slaves.

Core Idea
Combining testimony on ownership and admission is accepted for slaves but not for land due to the strength of possession.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines a nuance in testimony: when two witnesses express the same right differently—one by testifying to absolute ownership, the other by reporting the defendant's admission. The Hanafi school accepts this for a slave but rejects it for land or a female slave. The key is the difference in how possession indicates ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage describes a claim for a slave (عبد) where one witness testifies to absolute ownership (ملك مرسل) and another to the possessor's admission (إقرار).
2
State the ruling
For a slave, the testimony is accepted (تقبل). For a female slave (أمة) or land (ضيعة), it is not accepted (لا تقبل).
3
Understand the distinction
The reason (فرق) is that possession (يد) of a slave is weaker than possession of land or a female slave (umm walad context), so admission carries more weight.
Land possession is strong; slave possession is weak.
4
Note the reverse scenario
A claim based on admission is not valid because admission is not a cause of ownership; it only confirms a pre-existing right.
The majority of scholars hold that a claim of admission is not heard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ادعى عبداً فشهد أحدهما بملك مرسل والآخر بإقرار ذي اليد بملكيته للمدعي تقبل، ولو كان هذا في دعوى الأمة والضيعة لا تقبل والفرق فيها، وأما عكسها أعني ما إذا ادعى الإقرار فشهدا بالإنشاء فغير متصور شرعاً إذ لا تسمع الدعوى بالإقرار لما في البزازية معزياً إلى الذخيرة : ادعى أن له عليه كذا وأن العين الذي في يده له لما أنه أقر له به أو ابتدأ بدعوى الإقرار وقال إنه أقر أن هذا لي أو أقر أن لي عليه كذا قيل يصح وعامة المشايخ على أنه لا تصح الدعوى لعدم صلوح الإقرار للاستحقاق كالإقرار كاذباً فلا يصح الإقرار لإضافه الاستحقاق إليه بخلاف دعوى الإقرار من المدعى عليه على المدعي بأنه برهن على أنه أقر أنه لا حق له فيه أو بأنه ملك المدعي حيث تقبل وتمامه فيها، وسنتكلم عليها بأوضح من في الدعوى إن شاء الله تعالى ا هـ .
If one claims a slave, and one witness testifies to absolute ownership and the other testifies to the possessor's admission of ownership for the claimant, it is accepted. But if this were in a claim for a female slave or land, it would not be accepted, and there is a distinction. As for its opposite, meaning when one claims an admission and the witnesses testify to a direct creation (of ownership), it is not conceivable legally, because a claim based on admission is not heard, according to what is in al-Bazzaziyyah cited from al-Dhakhirah: 'If one claims that he has such-and-such against him and that the specific property in his hand belongs to him because he admitted it to him, or he initiates a claim of admission and says he admitted that this is mine or admitted that he owes me such-and-such, it is said to be valid, but the majority of scholars hold that the claim is not valid because admission is not suitable for establishing entitlement, like a false admission; so admission does not establish entitlement. Unlike a claim of admission by the defendant against the plaintiff that he proved that he admitted he has no right in it or that it is the plaintiff's property, where it is accepted. The full discussion is there, and we will speak on it more clearly in the chapter on claims, Allah willing.'
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a specific scenario in testimony: when one witness testifies that the claimant is the absolute owner of a slave, and another witness testifies that the person in possession (the defendant) admitted that the slave belongs to the claimant. The ruling is that this testimony is accepted because both witnesses agree on the essential fact—the claimant's ownership—though they express it differently: one directly, the other via the defendant's admission. However, if the claimed object is a female slave (umm walad?) or land, the testimony is not accepted. The distinction lies in the nature of claims: for a slave, possession is not as strong an indicator of ownership as for land, so admission by the possessor carries more weight. The author then discusses the reverse scenario: a claimant cannot base a lawsuit on an alleged admission because admission does not by itself create a right; it only confirms a pre-existing right. Thus, a claim 'I have a right because he admitted' is invalid, whereas if the defendant claims that the plaintiff admitted something, that can be valid. This shows the Hanafi nuance that admission is not a cause of ownership but evidence of it.

Key Terms
ملك مرسل (mulk mursal): Absolute ownership without specifying how it was acquired.
إقرار (iqrar): Admission by a party against his own interest; here it is the defendant's admission that the claimant owns the slave.
ذي اليد (dhi al-yad): The person in possession of the object (the defendant).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A slave (عبد) only.
B) Land (ضيعة) only.
C) A female slave (أمة) only.
D) Both a slave and land.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts combined testimony on ownership and admission for slaves but not for land or female slaves, due to the strength of possession.

The Condition of Agreement Between Witnesses: Wording vs. Meaning
Clarifying the condition of agreement between witnesses according to Abu Hanifa and his students

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Abu Hanifa's rule that witnesses must agree in both wording and meaning, distinguishing direct signification (wad') from implication (tadammun), and identify which testimonies are accepted or rejected in intra-Hanafi disputes.

Core Idea
Witnesses must agree in the direct meaning of their words, not just in legal consequence.

Testimony in court requires precision. When two witnesses describe the same event in different words, should the judge accept it if the meaning is the same? This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, on whether agreement in wording is necessary or just agreement in meaning. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the subtle distinction between direct signification (wad') and implication (tadammun), with concrete examples that guide the judge's decision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Notice that the base text says witnesses must agree 'in wording and meaning'. The commentator immediately flags this as Abu Hanifa's view, while Sahibayn require only agreement in meaning. This sets up the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
The dispute is about the threshold for accepting testimony as 'agreeing'.
2
Define 'agreement in wording' precisely
Agreement in wording means that the words convey the same meaning by direct linguistic assignment (wad'), not by secondary implication (tadammun). For example, 'one dirham' and 'two dirhams' have different direct meanings even though both imply some debt.
Wad' is the primary signification; tadammun is a necessary consequence.
3
Work through the dirham example
If multiple witnesses testify to different amounts (1,2,3,4,5 dirhams), Abu Hanifa rejects all because no two agree in wording. Sahibayn accept the minimum common amount (4 dirhams) as the agreed meaning. Note the practical difference in the judge's ruling.
The thamarah al-khilaf: different outcomes in court.
4
Distinguish synonyms from different words with same consequence
Synonyms like hibah and atiyyah (both meaning 'gift') are accepted because they share the same wad' meaning. But khaliyyah and bariyyah (both ending a marriage) have different direct meanings even though both imply separation; they are not accepted.
The key test: do the words share the same direct definition?
5
Apply the principle to other scenarios
Nikah and tazwij are synonyms (accepted). Testimony on the act of ghasb (usurpation) vs testimony on admission of ghasb are not agreeing because the direct meanings differ (one reports the act, the other reports a statement).
Always compare the direct signification, not the legal outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ويعتبر اتفاق الشاهدين لفظاً ومعنى أي عند أبي حنيفة رضي الله عنه، عندهما الاتفاق في المعنى والمراد باتفاقهما لفظاً تطابق لفظيهما على إفادة المعنى بطريق الوضع لا بطريق التضمن فلو ادعى على آخر مائة درهم فشهد واحد بدرهم وآخر بدرهمين وآخر بثلاثة وآخر بأربعة وآخر بخمسة لم تقبل عنده في شيء لعدم الموافقة لفظاً، وعندهما يقضى بأربعة. وكذا إن شهد أحدهما بألف والآخر بألفين لم تقبل عنده، وعندهما تقبل على الألف إذا كان المدعي يدعي ألفين وعلى هذا المائة والمائتان والطلقة والطلقتان والطلقة والثلاث؛ كذا في الكافي. وقد أشار بتفسير الموافقة إلى أنه لا يشترط أن يكون بعين ذلك اللفظ بل إما بعينه أو بمرادفه حتى لو شهد أحدهما بالهبة والآخر بالعطية تقبل، وبه اندفع ما في النهاية أن المطابقة في المعنى كافية للفرع المذكور لحصول المطابقة لفظاً ما لو شهد أحدهما بأنه قال لها أنت خلية وشهد الآخر بأنه قال لها أنت برية حيث لا تقبل لأنهما لفظان متباينان وإن اشتركا في لازم واحد وهو البينونة لأن معنى خلية غير معنى برية، وعلى هذا لو شهد أحدهما بالنكاح والآخر بالتزويج فإنها تقبل كما في المحيط. ولو شهد أحدهما بالغصب أو القتل والآخر بالإقرار به لا تقبل؛ ذكره الشارح .
His statement: 'And agreement of the two witnesses in wording and meaning is considered' — meaning according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), agreement in meaning suffices. What is meant by agreement in wording is that their two statements correspond in conveying the meaning by way of direct signification (bi-tariq al-wad'), not by way of implication (al-tadammun). So if one claims against another one hundred dirhams, and one witness testifies to one dirham, another to two, another to three, another to four, and another to five, it is not accepted according to him (Abu Hanifa) in any amount due to lack of agreement in wording. According to the two, they will judge for four dirhams. Likewise, if one witness testifies to one thousand and the other to two thousand, it is not accepted according to him, but according to the two it is accepted for one thousand if the claimant claims two thousand. And similarly for one hundred and two hundred, and one divorce and two divorces, and one divorce and three; as in al-Kafi. And he indicated by the interpretation of agreement that it is not required that it be with the exact same wording, but either with the same word or a synonym, such that if one witness testifies to hibah (gift) and the other to atiyyah (gift), it is accepted. And by this is refuted what is in al-Nihayah that agreement in meaning is sufficient for the mentioned branch, because agreement in wording is obtained here. (This is unlike) if one witness testifies that he said to her 'You are khaliyyah' and the other that he said 'You are bariyyah' – here it is not accepted, because they are two different words even though they share a common necessary consequence, which is separation (baynunah), because the meaning of khaliyyah is different from the meaning of bariyyah. On this basis, if one witness testifies to nikah and the other to tazwij, it is accepted, as in al-Muhit. And if one witness testifies to usurpation (ghasb) or killing (qatl) and the other to an admission thereof, it is not accepted; the commentator mentioned it.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi criteria for when testimony is considered 'agreeing' between two witnesses. The base text (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) states that witnesses must agree both in wording and meaning. The commentator clarifies: this is Abu Hanifa's view; his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that agreement in meaning alone suffices. Agreement in wording means that the words directly convey the same meaning (via وضع, the 'put' meaning), not via implication or necessary consequence. Example: if the claim is for 100 dirhams, and witnesses testify to different amounts (1,2,3,4,5), Abu Hanifa rejects all because the words differ; Sahibayn accept the minimum common amount (4 dirhams) because they agree in meaning (some debt). The same applies to 1000 vs 2000, one divorce vs two, etc. However, if the witnesses use synonyms like 'hibah' and 'atiyyah', Abu Hanifa accepts because they agree in meaning directly. The author refutes a statement in al-Nihayah that agreement in meaning is always sufficient; this is not true for words like 'khaliyyah' (a form of divorce) and 'bariyyah' (another form) which have different direct meanings though both entail separation. Thus they are not accepted. Conversely, 'nikah' and 'tazwij' are synonyms and accepted. Finally, testimony on the act of ghasb (usurpation) or qatl (killing) is not agreed with testimony on admission of the same act, because the direct meanings differ.

Key Terms
وضع (wad'): The direct, conventional meaning of a word as assigned by language.
تضمن (tadammun): Implication; a meaning that is necessarily included in the direct meaning but is not the primary sense.
مرادف (muradif): Synonym; a word that shares the same direct meaning as another.
خلية (khaliyyah): A form of divorce where the husband says 'You are free (from the marital bond)'; direct meaning: freedom from a confinement.
برية (bariyyah): A form of divorce where the husband says 'You are free (from any claim)'; direct meaning: freedom from a duty or connection.
هبة (hibah): Gift; a transfer of ownership without compensation.
عطية (atiyyah): Gift; a transfer of ownership without compensation.
نكاح (nikah): Marriage contract.
تزويج (tazwij): Marriage; the act of giving in marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is testimony with 'khaliyyah' and 'bariyyah' not accepted even though both end the marriage?
A) Because they are not valid forms of divorce.
B) Because they differ in their direct linguistic meaning (wad').
C) Because the judge must investigate further.
D) Because Sahibayn's view overrides.

Key Takeaway
Witnesses must agree in the direct meaning of their words; synonyms are acceptable, but words with different direct meanings even if same legal consequence are not.

Nine Cases Where Divergent Testimony Is Accepted
Listing exceptions where testimonies with different wording are considered agreeing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the nine specific cases where Hanafi law accepts testimonies with differing wording, and apply the underlying principle (agreement on core fact, difference only in description) to new scenarios.

Core Idea
Divergent testimonies are accepted if they agree on the core fact and differ only in description, not substance.

In the previous lesson, we learned the Hanafi rule that witnesses must agree in wording and meaning for their testimony to be valid. However, this rule has nine notable exceptions where apparent differences are overlooked. These cases demonstrate a deeper principle: the law looks to the essence of the testimony, not mere verbal consistency. In this lesson, we will examine each of these nine cases and understand their common rationale.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Recall that according to Abu Hanifa, witnesses must agree in wording and meaning for their testimony to be accepted. These nine cases are exceptions.
The general rule is the baseline; the exceptions show its limits.
2
Identify the nine cases
List each case from the passage with a short label: (1) debt vs. admission, (2) quality of wheat, (3) dinars of different origin, (4) hibah vs. atiyyah, (5) nikah vs. tazwij, (6) waqf share disagreement, (7) bay' al-wafa and its admission, (8) concubine ownership, (9) loan vs. deposit.
Memorizing the cases helps in quick application.
3
Analyze the common pattern
In each case, the witnesses agree on the core fact (e.g., a debt of 1000, a sale, ownership) and differ only on an extra description (quality, type, synonym, cause). The court accepts the testimony based on the lesser or common element.
The reasoning is that the extra description is not part of the essential claim.
4
Note the boundary condition
In the ninth case, if the claimant specifies one cause (e.g., loan), the testimony is not accepted because it would contradict his own claim. The divergence is only acceptable when the claim is general.
Specificity in the claim makes the witnesses' difference a contradiction.
5
Apply the principle
Test new scenarios: if witnesses differ on an attribute (price, date) but agree on the subject, acceptance is possible. If they differ on the subject itself, rejection applies.
The key is whether the difference is in a subsidiary detail or in the core fact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شهد أحدهما أن له عليه ألف درهم وشهد الآخر أنه أقر له بألف درهم تقبل ا هـ . وخرج عن ظاهر قول الإمام مسائل وإن أمكن رجوعها إليه في الحقيقة: الأولى ما في العمدة. الثانية ادعى كر حنطة فشهد أحدهما بأنها جيدة والآخر رديئة والدعوى بالأفضل يقضى بالأقل. الثالثة ادعى مائة دينار فقال أحدهما نيسابورية والآخر بخارية والمدعي يدعي النيسابورية وهو أجود يقضى بالبخارية بلا خلاف ينقل ومثله لو شهد أحدهما بألف بيض والآخر بألف سود والمدعي يدعي الأفضل تقبل على الأقل. ووجهه في المسائل الثلاث أنهما اتفقا على الكمية وانفرد أحدهما بزيادة وصف، ولو كان المدعي يدعي الأقل لا تقبل إلا إن وفق بالإبراء وتمامه في فتح القدير. الرابعة مسألة الهبة والعطية. الخامسة مسألة النكاح والتزويج وقدمناهما . السادسة شهد أحدهما أنه جعلها صدقة موقوفة أبداً على أن لزيد ثلث غلتها وشهد آخر أن لزيد نصفها تقبل على الثلث والباقي للمساكين؛ كذا في أوقاف الخصاف. السابعة ادعى أنه باع بيع الوفاء فإذا شهد أحدهما به والآخر بأن المشتري أقر بذلك تقبل كما في فتح القدير، ولا خصوصية لبيع الوفاء فإذا شهد أحدهما بالبيع والآخر بالإقرار به تقبل كما في جامع الفصولين ولا خصوصية للبيع بل كل قول كذلك بخلاف الفعل كما فيه أيضاً والنكاح كالفعل ا هـ . الثامنة شهد أحدهما أنها جاريته والآخر أنها كانت له تقبل كما في الفتح أيضاً. التاسعة ألفاً مطلقاً فشهد أحدهما على إقراره بألف قرض والآخر بألف وديعة تقبل، وإن ادعى أحد السببين لا تقبل لأنه أكذب شاهده؛ كذا في البزازية بخلاف ما إذا شهد أحدهما بألف
One witness testified that he owes him one thousand dirhams, and the other testified that he admitted to one thousand dirhams, it is accepted. And there are cases that depart from the apparent meaning of the Imam's statement, though they can be referred back to it in reality: First: what is in al-Umdah. Second: he claimed a kurr of wheat; one witness testified that it is good and the other that it is bad; the claim is for the better; judgment is given for the lesser. Third: he claimed one hundred dinars; one said they are Nishapuri and the other Bukhari; the claimant claims the Nishapuri which is superior; judgment is given for the Bukhari without reported disagreement. Similarly, if one witness testified to one thousand white (dirhams) and the other to one thousand black, and the claimant claims the better, it is accepted for the lesser. The reasoning in the three cases is that they agreed on the quantity and one added an extra description; if the claimant claimed the lesser, it is not accepted unless he is consistent with acquittal (ibra'). Full discussion in Fath al-Qadir. Fourth: the matter of hibah and atiyyah. Fifth: the matter of nikah and tazwij; we have presented them. Sixth: one witness testified that he made it an eternal waqf with Zaid having one third of its produce, and another testified that Zaid has half; it is accepted for the third and the rest for the poor; as in Awqaf al-Khassaf. Seventh: he claimed that he sold by bay' al-wafa; if one witness testifies to that and the other that the buyer admitted to it, it is accepted as in Fath al-Qadir; and there is no specificity for bay' al-wafa; if one testifies to the sale and the other to an admission thereof, it is accepted as in Jami' al-Fusulayn; and there is no specificity for sale; rather every statement (qawl) is like that, contrary to an action (fi'l), as also stated therein, and nikah is like an action. Eighth: one witness testified that she is his concubine, and the other that she was his; it is accepted as also in al-Fath. Ninth: one thousand in absolute terms; one witness testified to his admission of a thousand as a loan (qard) and the other to a thousand as a deposit (wadi'ah); it is accepted; but if he claims one of the two causes, it is not accepted because he would be discrediting his own witness; as in al-Bazzaziyyah, contrary to if one witness testifies to one thousand...
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage lists nine specific cases where testimonies that seem to differ are nevertheless accepted. The general principle from the earlier lesson is that witnesses must agree in wording and meaning according to Abu Hanifa. But these cases are exceptions where the difference is only in description or expression, not in the core fact. The first case: one witness testifies to a debt of 1000 dirhams, the other to an admission of the same; accepted because both affirm the debt. Second: claim for a kurr of wheat; witnesses differ on quality; the claimant claims the best quality, and the ruling is for the lower quality because both agree on quantity and only differ on an extra attribute; the burden of proof for the better quality falls on the claimant. Third: similar with Nishapuri and Bukhari dinars; judgment for the lesser (Bukhari). Fourth and fifth: synonyms (hibah/atiyyah, nikah/tazwij) are accepted. Sixth: waqf with differing shares; accepted for the lesser share (Zaid gets 1/3, the rest goes to the poor). Seventh: sale by wafa' and admission thereof; accepted. Eighth: 'she is his concubine' vs 'she was his'; accepted because both affirm his ownership at some point. Ninth: loan vs deposit; accepted because both affirm the debt of 1000. The author notes that if the claimant specifically claims one cause, then the testimony is not accepted because he contradicts his own claim by bringing a witness who says otherwise. This shows the nuance: the witnesses' difference is acceptable only if the claimant's claim is general; if specific, it becomes a contradiction.

Key Terms
بيع الوفاء (bay' al-wafa): A sale with the condition that the seller can repurchase the item upon returning the price; a form of security transaction.
إبراء (ibra'): Acquittal or discharge from a debt; here it means the claimant must release the defendant from the difference if he claims the lesser.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the sixth case (waqf with differing shares), why does the court accept the testimony that gives Zaid the smaller share (1/3) rather than the larger (1/2)?
A) Because the larger share contradicts the waqf's purpose.
B) Because the witnesses agree on the existence of a waqf, and the lesser share is the common element.
C) Because the second witness's testimony is weak.
D) Because the court follows the principle of caution in waqf matters.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi law accepts testimonies that differ in description but agree on the core fact, as illustrated by these nine specific cases.

Shahadat — Testimony

General Rule: Differing Wording, Same Meaning
Core rule and first exceptional case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that testimony is accepted when witnesses differ in wording but affirm the same legal fact, and distinguish it from other schools' requirements.

Core Idea
Testimony is accepted if the core fact is the same, even if the witnesses describe it differently.

In Islamic court proceedings, two witnesses must testify to the same fact. But what if one says 'I saw him give a loan of 1000 dirhams' and the other says 'I heard him admit he owes 1000 dirhams'? The Hanafi school, following Imam Abu Hanifa, accepts such testimony because the underlying fact—a debt of 1000 dirhams—is affirmed by both. This lesson unpacks that principle and its epistemological basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two testimonies
Read the first example: one witness testifies to a direct debt (anna lahu alayhi alf dirham), the other to an acknowledgment (annahu aqarra lahu bi-alf dirham). Write down the exact wording of each.
2
Isolate the core fact
Ask: what is the single legal fact both witnesses are trying to establish? Both affirm that the defendant owes the plaintiff 1000 dirhams. The different wording (debt vs acknowledgment) does not change this core.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule
State the ruling: the testimony is accepted (tuqbal). The reason is that agreement on the essential meaning suffices; verbal correspondence is not required.
This contrasts with some other schools that demand verbatim agreement.
4
Consider the epistemological weight
Note that the ruling comes from the apparently transmitted position (zahir al-riwayah) of Imam Abu Hanifa. It is considered sahih (valid) and mufta bihi.
Authority weight: zahir_riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشهادات باب الاختلاف في الشهادة شهد أحدهما أن له عليه ألف درهم وشهد الآخر أنه أقر له بألف درهم تقبل ا هـ . وخرج عن ظاهر قول الإمام مسائل وإن أمكن رجوعها إليه في الحقيقة: الأولى ما في العمدة. الثانية ادعى كر حنطة فشهد أحدهما بأنها جيدة والآخر رديئة والدعوى بالأفضل يقضى بالأقل. الثالثة ادعى مائة دينار فقال أحدهما نيسابورية والآخر بخارية والمدعي يدعي النيسابورية وهو أجود يقضى بالبخارية بلا خلاف ينقل ومثله لو شهد أحدهما بألف بيض والآخر بألف سود والمدعي يدعي الأفضل تقبل على الأقل. ووجهه في المسائل الثلاث أنهما اتفقا على الكمية وانفرد أحدهما بزيادة وصف، ولو كان المدعي يدعي الأقل لا تقبل إلا إن وفق بالإبراء وتمامه في فتح القدير.
Book of Testimonies, Chapter on Disagreement in Testimony: One witness testified that he has a debt of one thousand dirhams, and the other testified that he acknowledged owing one thousand dirhams; it is accepted. And from the apparent statement of Imam (Abu Hanifa) branch issues emerge, although they can be referred back to it in reality: The first is what is in al-Umdah. The second: He claimed a kurr of wheat, and one witness testified that it was good, the other that it was bad, and the claim is for the better; judgment is given for the lesser. The third: He claimed one hundred dinars, and one said they were Nishapuri, the other Bukhari, and the claimant claims the Nishapuri which is superior; judgment is given for the Bukhari, without transmitted disagreement. Similarly, if one witnesses for one thousand white dirhams and the other for one thousand black dirhams, and the claimant claims the superior, it is accepted for the lesser. And the reasoning in the three issues is that they agreed on the quantity, and one of them alone mentioned an increase in description. And if the claimant claimed the lesser, it is not accepted unless he harmonizes by discharging; and its completion is in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq opens the chapter on disagreements in testimony (Bab al-Ikhtilaf fi al-Shahada). The initial ruling states a fundamental principle: if one witness testifies to a direct debt (anna lahu alayhi alf dirham) and another testifies to an acknowledgment of that debt (annahu aqarra lahu bi-alf dirham), their testimony is accepted. This is because both essentially affirm the same legal fact—the existence of a debt—despite the different modes of expression. The author then notes that Imam Abu Hanifa's general principle covers many specific cases, which are enumerated. The first (mentioned only by reference to al-Umdah) is not detailed here. The second and third are illustrative: when the claimant claims a higher quality (good wheat, Nishapuri dinars, white dirhams) and witnesses disagree on quality, the court accepts the lesser (bad, Bukhari, black) because the witnesses agree on quantity (one kurr, one hundred dinars, one thousand dirhams) but one witness adds a favorable attribute. Accepting the lesser is a compromise that upholds both testimonies. The reasoning (wajh) is that they agreed on the quantity (al-kammiyya) and one witness alone added a description (wasf). If the claimant claims the lesser, the testimony is not accepted unless he can harmonize, e.g., by declaring that the extra description is a discharge (ibra'). The author refers to Fath al-Qadir for further details.

Key Terms
اختلاف في الشهادة (ikhtilāf fī al-shahāda): Disagreement between two witnesses in the wording or description of their testimony, as long as the core meaning is consistent.
تقبل (tuqbal): The testimony is legally accepted and considered valid for judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If one witness testifies that the defendant owes 1000 dirhams, and the other testifies that the defendant acknowledged owing 1000 dirhams, the testimony is:
A) Rejected due to conflict
B) Accepted because both affirm a debt
C) Accepted only if the defendant admits
D) Rejected because one is hearsay

Key Takeaway
Testimony is accepted if the witnesses agree on the core legal fact, even if they describe it differently.

Specific Cases: Disagreement on Quality
Three exceptional cases of quality disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that when witnesses agree on quantity but differ on quality, and the claimant claims the superior quality, the court accepts the lesser quality.

Core Idea
When witnesses agree on quantity but differ on quality, the court accepts the lesser quality.

The general rule from the previous lesson is applied to three specific examples where witnesses agree on the quantity of goods but describe the quality differently. For instance, one witness says the wheat is good, the other says it is bad, and the claimant claims the good. The Hanafi ruling is to accept the lesser quality because that is the common ground. This lesson works through these cases to solidify the principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
From the passage, list the three cases: (1) wheat of one kurr, one says good, one says bad; (2) one hundred dinars, one says Nishapuri, one says Bukhari; (3) one thousand dirhams, one says white, one says black. Note the claimant claims the superior quality in each.
2
Extract the common element (quantity)
In each case, both witnesses agree on the quantity: one kurr, one hundred dinars, one thousand dirhams. Write down that agreed quantity.
3
Identify the additional description
One witness adds a favorable description (good, Nishapuri, white) while the other describes the item as inferior (bad, Bukhari, black). This description is exclusive to one witness.
4
Apply the rule: accept the lesser
Since the witnesses agree on quantity but one alone adds a superior description, the court accepts the lesser quality (bad, Bukhari, black). The reasoning: they both affirm the quantity, and the extra description from one witness cannot override the other's testimony without corroboration.
5
Consider the claimant's claim
If the claimant claims the superior, the lesser is given. If the claimant claims the lesser, the testimony is not accepted unless the claimant harmonizes by discharging the superior (e.g., saying 'I discharge the extra quality').
This nuance is mentioned at the end of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
الكمية (al-kammiyya): The quantity or numerical amount that both witnesses agree upon.
الوصف (al-wasf): An additional attribute or quality mentioned by one witness only, which does not affect the core fact.
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Discharge; a declaration by the claimant that he releases the defendant from the superior quality claim.

Testimony on Waqf, Bay' al-Wafa, and Slave-Girl Ownership
A set of three rulings on testimony differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on testimony when witnesses differ on the share in a waqf or on the type of transaction, and apply the principle of accepting the lesser claim.

Core Idea
Testimony is accepted on the lesser claim when witnesses agree on the core fact but differ on the description.

In this lesson, we examine three specific cases from Hanafi jurisprudence where witnesses differ on the description of the same underlying fact. The Hanafi school holds that such testimony is accepted, provided the witnesses agree on the core subject matter. We will see how the principle applies to waqf, conditional sale (bay' al-wafa), and ownership of a slave-girl.

Guided Path
1
Identify each case
List the three cases: waqf (endowment) where witnesses differ on Zayd's share; bay' al-wafa (sale with repurchase right) where one witnesses the sale and the other witnesses acknowledgment; and slave-girl ownership where one says she is currently owned and the other says she was owned before.
2
State the witness disagreement
For each case, note exactly what the witnesses differ on: amount of share, type of transaction, or temporal ownership status.
3
Note the ruling
In all cases, the testimony is accepted (tuqbal). The judge rules based on the lesser claim: for waqf, the smaller share (1/3); for bay' al-wafa, the sale is upheld; for the slave-girl, current ownership is established.
4
Explain the principle
The core fact is agreed upon; the difference is in description or attribute. Hanafis consider this acceptable because the essence is the same.
5
Apply to similar cases
The principle extends to any verbal transaction (aqwal), but not to actions (af'al) like marriage or physical acts.

في أوقاف الخصاف. السابعة ادعى أنه باع بيع الوفاء فإذا شهد أحدهما به والآخر بأن المشتري أقر بذلك تقبل كما في فتح القدير، ولا خصوصية لبيع الوفاء فإذا شهد أحدهما بالبيع والآخر بالإقرار به تقبل كما في جامع الفصولين ولا خصوصية للبيع بل كل قول كذلك بخلاف الفعل كما فيه أيضاً والنكاح كالفعل ا هـ . الثامنة شهد أحدهما أنها جاريته والآخر أنها كانت له تقبل كما في الفتح أيضاً. التاسعة ألفاً مطلقاً فشهد أحدهما على إقراره بألف قرض والآخر بألف وديعة تقبل، وإن ادعى أحد السببين لا تقبل لأنه أكذب شاهده؛ كذا في البزازية بخلاف ما إذا شهد أحدهما بألف ادعى رملي : ذكر في باب اختلاف الشهادات من شهادات الجامع : وليس الاختلاف بين الشاهدين بمنزلة الاختلاف بين الدعوى والشهادة لأن شهادة الشاهدين ينبغي أن تكون كل واحدة منهما مطابقة للأخرى في اللفظ الذي لا يوجب خللاً في المعنى، أما المطابقة بين الدعوى والشهادة فينبغي أن يكون في المعنى خاصة ولا عبرة للفظ حتى لو ادعى الغصب وشهد أحدهما على الغصب والآخر على الإقرار بالغصب لا تقبل ولو شهدا على الإقرار بالغصب تقبل. وتمامه في الفصول العمادية اهـ. وفي جامع الفصولين للمحيط : وصور المسألة وإذا شهد أحدهما على إقراره أنه قتله عمداً بالسيف وشهد الآخر على إقراره أنه قتله عمداً بالسكين فقال ولي القتيل إنه أقر بما قالا ولكنه والله ما قتله إلا بالسيف أو قال صدقا جميعاً لكنه والله ما قتله إلا بالرمح فهذا كله سواء ويقتص من القاتل اهـ. تدبره. هذا وقد صرح أيضاً في شرح الغرر بالمسألة فقال بعدما ذكر المسألة التي هنا : بخلاف ما إذا شهدا بالإقرار به حيث تقبل اهـ . قوله : (ولا) خصوصية لبيع الوفاء الخ) يدخل فيه ما في العمدة المسألة الأولى قوله : (منها وهو قرض والآخر بألف وديعة فإنها لا تقبل منها أيضاً. العاشرة ادعى الإبراء فشهد أحدهما به والآخر على أنه وهبه أو تصدق عليه أو حلله جاز بخلاف ما إذا شهد أحدهما على الهبة والآخر على الصدقة لا تقبل؛ كذا في البزازية الحادية عشرة ادعى الهبة فشهد أحدهما بالبراءة والآخر بالهبة أو أنه حلله جاز. الثانية عشرة ادعى الكفيل الهبة فشهد أحدهما بها والآخر بالإبراء جاز ويثبت الإبراء لا الهبة لأنه أقلهما فلا يرجع الكفيل على الأصيل وهما في البزازية.
Fourth: Issue of gift and donation. Fifth: Issue of marriage and matrimony, and we have presented them. Sixth: One witness testified that he made it a perpetual charitable endowment (waqf) on the condition that Zayd gets one third of its produce, and another witness testified that Zayd gets half; it is accepted for the third, and the rest is for the poor; thus in Awqaf al-Khassaf. Seventh: He claimed that he sold with a sale of wafa (buy-back agreement). So if one witness testifies to that and the other that the buyer acknowledged it, it is accepted, as in Fath al-Qadir. And there is no specificity for sale of wafa; if one witnesses to the sale and the other to the acknowledgment thereof, it is accepted, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And there is no specificity for sale; rather, every statement is like that, unlike an action, as mentioned therein, and marriage is like an action. Eighth: One witness testified that she is his slave-girl, and the other that she used to be his; it is accepted, as in Fath also. Ninth: For an absolute thousand, if one witnesses to his acknowledgment of a thousand as a loan, and the other as a deposit, it is accepted. But if he claims one of the two causes, it is not accepted because he has belied his witness; thus in al-Bazzaziyya, contrary to when one witnesses to a thousand... al-Ramli: It is mentioned in Bab Ikhtilaf al-Shahadat from Shahadat al-Jami': The difference between the two witnesses is not like the difference between the claim and the testimony, because each of the two testimonies should be matching the other in wording that does not cause a defect in meaning. As for the matching between claim and testimony, it should be specifically in meaning, and no attention is paid to wording. Thus, if he claimed usurpation, and one witnesses to the usurpation and the other to the acknowledgment of the usurpation, it is not accepted. But if they both witnesses to the acknowledgment of usurpation, it is accepted. And its completion is in al-Fusul al-Imadiyya. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: He claimed murder and one witnessed to it and another that he acknowledged it, it is rejected because acknowledgment is repeated, not the murder. al-Ramli said in his gloss: I say: if they agreed on testimony on acknowledgment, it is accepted as is apparent, and it is stated in al-Tatarakhaniyya from al-Muhit. He said after referencing al-Muhit: The form of the issue: if one witnesses to his acknowledgment that he killed him intentionally with a sword, and the other witnesses to his acknowledgment that he killed him intentionally with a knife, and the heir of the slain says: he acknowledged what they both said, but by Allah he only killed him with the sword, or he says: both are truthful, but by Allah he only killed him with a spear; all this is the same, and the killer is executed. His saying: (And there is no specificity for sale of wafa) includes what is in al-Umdah, the first issue. His saying: (from them, and it is) loan and the other with a thousand deposit: then it is not accepted from it also. Tenth: He claimed discharge, so one witness testified to it and the other that he gifted to him or gave charity to him or made it permissible; it is valid. Contrary to when one witnesses to the gift and the other to the charity, it is not accepted; thus in al-Bazzaziyya. Eleventh: He claimed a gift, so one witness testified to the discharge and the other to the gift or that he made it permissible; it is valid. Twelfth: The guarantor claimed a gift, so one witness testified to it and the other to the discharge; it is valid, and the discharge is established, not the gift, because it is the lesser of the two, so the guarantor cannot recourse against the principal; both are in al-Bazzaziyya.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on testimony disagreements, presenting a series of specific cases (masail) from fourth to twelfth. The fourth and fifth are only referenced (gift/donation and marriage) as previously discussed. The sixth deals with waqf: one witness says Zayd gets one-third, the other says half; the testimony is accepted for the lower share (one-third), and the remainder goes to the poor. The seventh extends the principle to a sale with option of repurchase (bay' al-wafa): if one witnesses the sale itself and the other witnesses the buyer's acknowledgment, the testimony is accepted. The author stresses that this is not limited to bay' al-wafa but applies to any verbal transaction, unlike actions (like marriage). The eighth case: one says she is his slave-girl, the other says she was his before; accepted. The ninth case: one witnesses to an acknowledgment of a loan of 1000, the other to a deposit (wadi'a) of 1000; if the claimant does not specify a cause, the testimony is accepted for the lesser (deposit? Actually the text says it is accepted, but then adds a condition: if the claimant claims one specific cause, it is not accepted because he belies one witness. The editors add a quote from al-Ramli distinguishing between differences among witnesses and between claim and testimony. A long citation explains that if both witnesses testify to an acknowledgment (e.g., of murder with different weapons), it is accepted and the killer is executed. The tenth to twelfth cases address discharge (ibra') and gift (hiba). The principle is that when witnesses give different legal descriptions for the same right, the testimony is accepted for the lesser right (the one with lower legal effect). For example, if one says ibra' (discharge) and another says hiba (gift), the ibra' is accepted because it is the lesser (it simply cancels the debt) while hiba involves transfer of ownership. The twelfth case involves a guarantor (kafil) claiming a gift from the creditor, but the witnesses differ; the discharge is established, so the guarantor cannot recourse against the principal debtor.

Key Terms
بيع الوفاء (bay' al-wafā'): A conditional sale where the seller retains the right to repurchase; treated as a security transaction.
الإبراء (al-ibrā'): A unilateral discharge or release from a debt, canceling the obligation.
الهبة (al-hiba): A gift, transferring ownership without compensation.
الكفيل (al-kafīl): A guarantor who becomes liable for the debt of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the waqf case, if one witness says Zayd gets 1/3 and another says 1/2, the testimony is:
A) Rejected because the shares differ
B) Accepted for 1/2 (the greater share)
C) Accepted for 1/3 (the lesser share)
D) Accepted only if the donor confirms 1/3

Key Takeaway
When witnesses agree on the core fact but differ on a description (e.g., share, legal cause), Hanafis accept the testimony for the lesser claim.

Shahadat — Testimony
advanced
15 min
Testimony on Loan vs Deposit and the Principle of Acknowledgment
A case on testimony for a fixed amount with different causes, plus a general principle on acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that testimony on a fixed amount is accepted despite different legal causes (loan vs deposit) and distinguish between differences among witnesses and differences between claim and testimony.

Core Idea
For a fixed amount, testimony is accepted if witnesses differ on the cause (e.g., loan vs deposit) but rejected if the claimant specifies a cause that contradicts one witness.

This lesson delves into a more complex scenario: when the amount is agreed (e.g., 1000 dirhams) but witnesses differ on the legal cause (loan vs deposit). The Hanafi school accepts the testimony, but with a crucial condition: the claimant must not specify a cause. We also explore the key distinction between disagreement among witnesses versus disagreement between the claimant and the witnesses, extracted from the commentary of al-Ramli.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the disagreement
The case (ninth) involves a claim for 1000 dirhams. One witness testifies that the defendant acknowledged it as a loan (qard), the other as a deposit (wadi'a).
2
State the ruling with condition
If the claimant does not specify the cause, the testimony is accepted. If he specifies one cause (e.g., loan), it is rejected because he belies the other witness.
3
Analyze the principle from al-Ramli
Al-Ramli distinguishes between differences among witnesses (acceptable if meaning matches) and differences between claim and testimony (must match in meaning, not wording). Example: if claimant claims usurpation, and one witness testifies to usurpation, the other to acknowledgment of usurpation, it is rejected.
4
Apply to murder case
In a murder case, if one witness testifies to the act, another to acknowledgment of the act, it is rejected because the act itself cannot be acknowledged repeatedly. But if both testify to the acknowledgment, it is accepted.
5
Identify the practical outcome
The judge will rule on the lesser of the two claims. For loan vs deposit, the deposit is the lesser (since it does not require repayment? Actually deposit is amanah, loan is debt; the lesser liability is the deposit because it is not due until demand? In practice, the court may rule for the deposit or the loan? The text says 'tuqbal' but does not specify which. By the principle of accepting the lesser claim, it would be the deposit (wadi'a) as it is a trust, not a debt. However, the text does not clarify; the general rule suggests the lesser prevails.
The source notes some uncertainty, but the dominant view is to rule for the lesser (deposit).

Shahadat — Testimony

Testimony on Discharge and Gift (Ibra, Hiba) and Guarantor Cases
Final rulings on testimony for discharge and gift, including a guarantor scenario

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when witnesses differ on whether a debt was discharged (ibra) or gifted (hiba), the testimony is accepted for the lesser (ibra), and determine the effect on a guarantor's liability.

Core Idea
When witnesses differ on the mode of extinguishing a debt (ibra vs hiba), the lesser claim (ibra) prevails, and the guarantor is freed from recourse.

This lesson covers three final cases (tenth, eleventh, twelfth) from the passage, all dealing with discharge (ibra) and gift (hiba). The principle established is that when witnesses give different legal descriptions for the same right, the testimony is accepted for the legal effect that is lesser. We also see the application to a guarantor: if the creditor's debt is discharged, the guarantor cannot recourse against the principal debtor.

Guided Path
1
Identify each case
Case 10: Claimant alleges discharge (ibra), witnesses differ on whether it was ibra, hiba, sadaqa, or tahlil. Case 11: Claimant alleges hiba, witnesses differ on ibra or tahlil. Case 12: Guarantor (kafil) claims the principal debtor gave a gift to the creditor, witnesses differ on ibra vs hiba.
2
State the ruling for each case
All cases: testimony is accepted (jaza). In case 10, if one witnesses ibra and the other hiba/sadaqa/tahlil, accepted. In case 11, if one witnesses ibra and the other hiba/tahlil, accepted. In case 12, if one witnesses ibra and the other hiba, accepted, but ibra is established (not hiba).
3
Explain the principle of the lesser claim
Ibra (discharge) is the lesser because it simply cancels the debt, while hiba transfers ownership and requires acceptance. So when witnesses differ, the testimony is accepted for the lesser (ibra).
4
Apply to the guarantor case
The guarantor claims the debt was gifted to the creditor, but the witnesses show it was discharged. Since ibra is established, the principal debtor is freed, and the guarantor cannot recourse against him because there was no gift that would create a claim for the guarantor.
5
Contrast with rejected case
If one witnesses to hiba and the other to sadaqa (both transfers of ownership), the testimony is NOT accepted because neither is a discharge; they both affirm a transfer, but differ on the type of transfer. The principle of accepting the lesser does not apply because neither is lesser in the sense of extinguishing the debt.

Fourth: Issue of gift and donation. Fifth: Issue of marriage and matrimony, and we have presented them. And there is no specificity for sale; rather, every statement is like that, unlike an action, as mentioned therein, and marriage is like an action. Eighth: One witness testified that she is his slave-girl, and the other that she used to be his; it is accepted, as in Fath also. Ninth: For an absolute thousand, if one witnesses to his acknowledgment of a thousand as a loan, and the other as a deposit, it is accepted. As for the matching between claim and testimony, it should be specifically in meaning, and no attention is paid to wording. Thus, if he claimed usurpation, and one witnesses to the usurpation and the other to the acknowledgment of the usurpation, it is not accepted. But if they both witnesses to the acknowledgment of usurpation, it is accepted. And its completion is in al-Fusul al-Imadiyya. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: He claimed murder and one witnessed to it and another that he acknowledged it, it is rejected because acknowledgment is repeated, not the murder. al-Ramli said in his gloss: I say: if they agreed on testimony on acknowledgment, it is accepted as is apparent, and it is stated in al-Tatarakhaniyya from al-Muhit. He said after referencing al-Muhit: The form of the issue: if one witnesses to his acknowledgment that he killed him intentionally with a sword, and the other witnesses to his acknowledgment that he killed him intentionally with a knife, and the heir of the slain says: he acknowledged what they both said, but by Allah he only killed him with the sword, or he says: both are truthful, but by Allah he only killed him with a spear; all this is the same, and the killer is executed. His saying: (And there is no specificity for sale of wafa) includes what is in al-Umdah, the first issue. His saying: (from them, and it is) loan and the other with a thousand deposit: then it is not accepted from it also. Tenth: He claimed discharge, so one witness testified to it and the other that he gifted to him or gave charity to him or made it permissible; it is valid. Contrary to when one witnesses to the gift and the other to the charity, it is not accepted; thus in al-Bazzaziyya. Eleventh: He claimed a gift, so one witness testified to the discharge and the other to the gift or that he made it permissible; it is valid. Twelfth: The guarantor claimed a gift, so one witness testified to it and the other to the discharge; it is valid, and the discharge is established, not the gift, because it is the lesser of the two, so the guarantor cannot recourse against the principal; both are in al-Bazzaziyya.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on testimony disagreements, presenting a series of specific cases (masail) from fourth to twelfth. The fourth and fifth are only referenced (gift/donation and marriage) as previously discussed. The sixth deals with waqf: one witness says Zayd gets one-third, the other says half; the testimony is accepted for the lower share (one-third), and the remainder goes to the poor. The seventh extends the principle to a sale with option of repurchase (bay' al-wafa): if one witnesses the sale itself and the other witnesses the buyer's acknowledgment, the testimony is accepted. The author stresses that this is not limited to bay' al-wafa but applies to any verbal transaction, unlike actions (like marriage). The eighth case: one says she is his slave-girl, the other says she was his before; accepted. The ninth case: one witnesses to an acknowledgment of a loan of 1000, the other to a deposit (wadi'a) of 1000; if the claimant does not specify a cause, the testimony is accepted for the lesser (deposit? Actually the text says it is accepted, but then adds a condition: if the claimant claims one specific cause, it is not accepted because he belies one witness. The editors add a quote from al-Ramli distinguishing between differences among witnesses and between claim and testimony. A long citation explains that if both witnesses testify to an acknowledgment (e.g., of murder with different weapons), it is accepted and the killer is executed. The tenth to twelfth cases address discharge (ibra') and gift (hiba). The principle is that when witnesses give different legal descriptions for the same right, the testimony is accepted for the lesser right (the one with lower legal effect). For example, if one says ibra' (discharge) and another says hiba (gift), the ibra' is accepted because it is the lesser (it simply cancels the debt) while hiba involves transfer of ownership. The twelfth case involves a guarantor (kafil) claiming a gift from the creditor, but the witnesses differ; the discharge is established, so the guarantor cannot recourse against the principal debtor.

Key Terms
الإبراء (al-ibrā'): A unilateral discharge or release from a debt, canceling the obligation.
الهبة (al-hiba): A gift, transferring ownership without compensation.
الكفيل (al-kafīl): A guarantor who becomes liable for the debt of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In case 10, if one witness testifies to a gift (hiba) and the other to charity (sadaqa), the testimony is:
A) Accepted and the court rules for sadaqa
B) Accepted for hiba
C) Rejected because neither is ibra
D) Accepted for whichever is lesser

Key Takeaway
When witnesses differ on the mode of extinguishing a debt, the testimony is accepted for the lesser (ibra), and if the lesser is ibra, the guarantor cannot recourse.

Shahadat — Testimony

Testimonies on Acknowledgments and Property (Cases 13-19)
Thirteenth to nineteenth cases on testimonies about acknowledgments, birth, property, and permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for accepting testimony when witnesses agree on the core fact but differ on peripheral details, and identify the exception for inferred permission.

Core Idea
Testimony is accepted if witnesses agree on the core fact; disagreement on peripheral details does not invalidate.

This lesson examines a series of rulings from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on shahadat. The cases illustrate a consistent principle: as long as the witnesses agree on the fundamental occurrence (e.g., acknowledgment, birth, permission), differences in specifics such as the gender of a child or the language of acknowledgment do not render the testimony inadmissible. However, there is a critical exception when one witness testifies to explicit permission and the other merely infers permission from conduct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core fact
In each case, determine what fact both witnesses agree on. For example, in case 13, both acknowledge that the defendant took the slave; in case 15, both confirm the child was born from the husband.
2
Identify the point of disagreement
Note the specific detail on which the witnesses differ: e.g., whether the slave was taken or deposited (case 13), the gender of the child (case 16), or the language of acknowledgment (case 19).
3
Apply the rule for peripheral differences
Since the disagreement is not on the core fact but on a peripheral attribute, the testimony is accepted (tuqbal).
4
Note the exception
In case 18, if one witness testifies to explicit permission (إذن صريح) and the other to conduct (رآه يشتري ويبيع فسكت), the testimony is not accepted because the second witness infers permission rather than testifying to it directly.
This exception highlights that testimony must be based on direct observation of the fact, not inference.
5
Distinguish language differences
Case 19 shows that disagreement on language (Arabic vs. Persian) is accepted for wealth acknowledgment, but not for divorce (as will be seen in the next lesson). This distinction stems from the precision required in divorce wording.

الثالثة عشرة شهد أحدهما على إقراره أنه أخذ العبد والآخر على إقراره بأنه أودعه منه هذا العبد تقبل لاتفاقهما على الإقرار بالأخذ الرابعة عشرة شهد أحدهما أنه غصبه منه والآخر أن فلاناً أودع منه هذا العبد يقضى للمدعي ولا يقبل من المدعى عليه بينة بعده لأن الشاهدين شهدا على إقراره بالملك. الخامسة عشرة شهد أحدهما أنها ولدت منه والآخر أنها حبلت منه تقبل . السادسة عشرة شهد أحدهما أنها ولدت منه ذكراً وقال الآخر أنثى تقبل ؛ ؛ كذا في البزازية . السابعة عشرة شهد أحدهما أنه أقر أن الدار له والآخر أنه سكن فيها تقبل. الثامنة عشرة أنكر إذن عبده فشهد أحدهما على أنه أذن له في الثياب والآخر على أنه أذن له في الطعام تقبل بخلاف ما إذا قال أحدهما إنه أذنه صريحاً وقال الآخر رآه يشتري ويبيع فسكت لا تقبل . التاسعة عشرة اختلف شاهدا الإقرار بالمال في كونه أقر بالعربية أو بالفارسية تقبل بخلافه في الطلاق. العشرون شهد أحدهما بأنه قال لعبده أنت حر وقال الآخر قال له آزدي تقبل. الحادية والعشرون قال لامرأته إن كلمت فلاناً فأنت طالق فشهد أحدهما أنها كلمته غدوة والآخر عشية طلقت. الثانية والعشرون إن طلقتك فعبده حر فقال أحدهما طلقها اليوم وقال الآخر أنه طلقها أمس يقع الطلاق والعتاق الثالثة والعشرون شهد أحدهما أنه طلقها ثلاثاً البتة والآخر أنه طلقها ثنتين ألبتة يقضى بطلقتين ويملك الرجعة؛ ذكره في المنتقى عن هشام عن محمد بخلاف ما إذا شهد أحدهما أنه أعتق كله والآخر أنه أعتق نصفه لا تقبل، وعلى هذا ففرق بين الطلقة والطلقتين وبين هذه، والفرق أنهما هنا اتفقا على البينونة لفظاً ومعنى وإن اختلفا في العدد بخلاف تلك. وفي العيون لأبي الليث هشام عن محمد في رجل تحته أمة فأعتقت فشهد عليه شاهدان فقال أحدهما أشهد أنك طلقتها وهي أمة ثلاثاً وشهد الآخر أنه طلقها بعد ما أعتقت ثلاثاً قال : هما تطليقتان فيملك الرجعة لأن الثلاث التي شهد بها في حال الرق واحدة منهما ليست بشيء. ولو شهد

He said: they are two divorces, and he retains the right to take her back because the three that were witnessed during slavery, one of them is nothing. And if they witnessed...
